.navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Jainism" title="Template:Jainism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Jainism" title="Template talk:Jainism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Jainism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Jainism</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/dʒ/: 'j' in 'jam'">dʒ</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/əm/: 'm' in 'rhythm'">əm</span></span>/</a></span></span>) also known as <b>Jain Dharma</b>, is an ancient <a href="/wiki/Indian_religions" title="Indian religions">Indian religion</a>. Jainism traces its spiritual ideas and history through the succession of twenty-four <a href="/wiki/Tirthankara" title="Tirthankara">Tirthankaras</a> (supreme preachers of <i>Dharma</i>), with the first in the current time cycle being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rishabhadeva" title="Rishabhadeva">Rishabhadeva</a>, whom the tradition holds to have lived millions of years ago, the twenty-third <i>tirthankara</i> <a href="/wiki/Parshvanatha" title="Parshvanatha">Parshvanatha</a>, whom historians date to the 9th century BCE, and the twenty-fourth <i>tirthankara</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81v%C4%ABra" title="Mahāvīra">Mahavira</a>, around 600 BCE. Jainism is considered to be an eternal <i><a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a></i> with the <i>tirthankaras</i> guiding every time cycle of the <a href="/wiki/Jain_cosmology" title="Jain cosmology">cosmology</a>. The three main pillars of Jainism are <i><a href="/wiki/Ahimsa_in_Jainism" title="Ahimsa in Jainism">ahiṃsā</a></i> (non-violence), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anek%C4%81ntav%C4%81da" title="Anekāntavāda">anekāntavāda</a></i> (non-absolutism), and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aparigraha" title="Aparigraha">aparigraha</a></i> (asceticism).
</p><p>Jain monks, after positioning themselves in the sublime state of soul consciousness, take five main vows: <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahi%E1%B9%83s%C4%81" title="Ahiṃsā">ahiṃsā</a></i> (non-violence), <i><a href="/wiki/Satya" title="Satya">satya</a></i> (truth), <i><a href="/wiki/Achourya" title="Achourya">asteya</a></i> (not stealing), <i><a href="/wiki/Brahmacharya" title="Brahmacharya">brahmacharya</a></i> (chastity), and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aparigraha" title="Aparigraha">aparigraha</a></i> (non-possessiveness). These principles have affected Jain culture in many ways, such as leading to a predominantly <a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism" title="Vegetarianism">vegetarian</a> lifestyle. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasparopagraho_J%C4%ABv%C4%81n%C4%81m" title="Parasparopagraho Jīvānām">Parasparopagraho jīvānām</a></i> (the function of souls is to help one another) is the faith's motto, and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E1%B9%86am%C5%8Dk%C4%81ra_mantra" title="Ṇamōkāra mantra">Ṇamōkāra mantra</a></i> is its most common and basic prayer.
</p><p>Jainism is one of the world's oldest religions in practice to this day. It has two major ancient sub-traditions, <a href="/wiki/Digambara" title="Digambara">Digambaras</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Av%C4%93t%C4%81mbara" title="Śvētāmbara">Śvētāmbaras</a>, with different views on ascetic practices, gender, and the texts that can be considered canonical; both have <a href="/wiki/Mendicant" title="Mendicant">mendicants</a> supported by <a href="/wiki/Laity" title="Laity">laypersons</a> (<i>śrāvakas</i> and <i>śrāvikas</i>). The Śvētāmbara tradition in turn has three sub-traditions: Mandirvāsī, Deravasi, and Sthānakavasī.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The religion has between four and five million followers, known as <b>Jains</b>, who reside mostly in <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_India" title="Jainism in India">India</a>. Outside India, some of the largest communities are in <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_Canada" title="Jainism in Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_Europe" title="Jainism in Europe">Europe</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_the_United_States" title="Jainism in the United States">United States</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_Japan" title="Jainism in Japan">Japan</a> hosting a fast-growing community of converts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Major festivals include <i><a href="/wiki/Paryushana" title="Paryushana">Paryushana</a></i> and <i>Das Lakshana</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Jain_festivals#Ashtanhika_Parv" title="Jain festivals">Ashtanika</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mahavir_Janma_Kalyanak" title="Mahavir Janma Kalyanak">Mahavir Janma Kalyanak</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Akshaya_Tritiya" title="Akshaya Tritiya">Akshaya Tritiya</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Diwali_(Jainism)" title="Diwali (Jainism)">Dipawali</a></i>.
</p><p>Estimates for the <a href="/wiki/List_of_religious_populations#Adherents_in_2020" title="List of religious populations">population of Jains</a> put them at around five million in the world, with the towering majority situated in India, where they number around 4,5 million as per the 2011 census.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs_and_philosophy">Beliefs and philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Beliefs and philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jain_philosophy" title="Jain philosophy">Jain philosophy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ahimsa_Jainism.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1506" data-file-width="714" decoding="async" height="316" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Ahimsa_Jainism.svg/150px-Ahimsa_Jainism.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Ahimsa_Jainism.svg/225px-Ahimsa_Jainism.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Ahimsa_Jainism.svg/300px-Ahimsa_Jainism.svg.png 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ahimsa_Jainism.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The hand symbolizes <a href="/wiki/Ahimsa_in_Jainism" title="Ahimsa in Jainism"><i>Ahiṃsā</i></a>, the wheel <i><a href="/wiki/Dharmachakra" title="Dharmachakra">dharmachakra</a></i>, the resolve to halt <i>saṃsāra</i> (transmigration).</div></div></div>
<p>Jainism is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transtheistic" title="Transtheistic">transtheistic</a> and forecasts that the universe evolves without violating the law of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substance_dualism" title="Substance dualism">substance dualism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYandell1999243_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYandell1999243-3">[3]</a></sup> and the actual realization of this principle plays out through the phenomena of both <a href="/wiki/Psychophysical_parallelism" title="Psychophysical parallelism">parallelism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Interactionism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Interactionism (philosophy of mind)">interactionism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinha194420_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinha194420-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Dravya_.28Ontological_facts.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Dravya_(Ontological_facts)"><i>Dravya</i> (Ontological facts)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Dravya (Ontological facts)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dravya" title="Dravya">Dravya</a></div>
<p><i>Dravya</i> means substances or entity in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes1996118–119_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes1996118–119-5">[5]</a></sup> The universe is made up of six eternal substances: sentient beings or souls (<i><a href="/wiki/J%C4%ABva_(Jainism)" title="Jīva (Jainism)">jīva</a></i>), non-sentient substance or matter (<i><a href="/wiki/Pudgala" title="Pudgala">pudgala</a></i>), the principle of motion (<i><a href="/wiki/Dharma_(Jainism)" title="Dharma (Jainism)">dharma</a></i>), the principle of rest (<i><a href="/wiki/Adharma" title="Adharma">adharma</a></i>), space (<i><a href="/wiki/%C4%80k%C4%81%C5%9Ba_(Jainism)" title="Ākāśa (Jainism)">ākāśa</a></i>), and time (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C4%81la_(time)" title="Kāla (time)">kāla</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes1996118–119_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes1996118–119-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENemicandraBalbir20101_of_Introduction_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENemicandraBalbir20101_of_Introduction-6">[6]</a></sup> The last five are united as the <i>ajiva</i> (non-living).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes1996118–119_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes1996118–119-5">[5]</a></sup> Jains distinguish a substance from a complex body, or thing, by declaring the former a simple indestructible element, while the latter is a compound made of one or more substances that can be destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChampat_Rai_Jain191715_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChampat_Rai_Jain191715-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Tattva_.28Soteriological_facts.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Tattva_(Soteriological_facts)"><i>Tattva</i> (Soteriological facts)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Tattva (Soteriological facts)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tattva_(Jainism)" title="Tattva (Jainism)">Tattva (Jainism)</a></div>
<p><i>Tattva</i> connotes reality or truth in Jain philosophy and is the framework for salvation. According to Digambara Jains, there are seven <i>tattvas</i>: the sentient (<i><a href="/wiki/J%C4%ABva_(Jainism)" title="Jīva (Jainism)">jiva</a></i> or living), the insentient (<i><a href="/wiki/Ajiva" title="Ajiva">ajiva</a></i> or non-living), the karmic influx to the soul (<i><a href="/wiki/Asrava" title="Asrava">Āsrava</a></i>, which is a mix of living and non-living), the bondage of karmic particles to the soul (<i><a href="/wiki/Bandha_(Jainism)" title="Bandha (Jainism)">Bandha</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925188–190_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925188–190-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1980219–228_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1980219–228-9">[9]</a></sup> the stoppage of karmic particles (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%83vara" title="Saṃvara">Saṃvara</a></i>), the wiping away of past karmic particles (<i><a href="/wiki/Nirjara" title="Nirjara">Nirjarā</a></i>), and the liberation (<i><a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">Moksha</a></i>). Śvētāmbaras add two further <i>tattvas</i>, namely good karma (<i>Punya</i>) and bad karma (<i>Paapa</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925177–187_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925177–187-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998151_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998151-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200296–98_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200296–98-12">[12]</a></sup> The true insight in Jain philosophy is considered as "faith in the <i>tattvas</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998151_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998151-11">[11]</a></sup> The spiritual goal in Jainism is to reach <i>moksha</i> for ascetics, but for most Jain laypersons, it is to accumulate good karma that leads to better rebirth and a step closer to liberation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBailey2012108_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBailey2012108-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong201318,_98–100_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong201318,_98–100-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Pramana_.28Epistemological_facts.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pramana_(Epistemological_facts)"><i>Pramana</i> (Epistemological facts)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Pramana (Epistemological facts)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jain_epistemology" title="Jain epistemology">Jain epistemology</a></div>
<p>Jain philosophy accepts three reliable means of knowledge (<i><a href="/wiki/Pramana" title="Pramana">pramana</a></i>). It holds that correct knowledge is based on perception (<i>pratyaksa</i>), inference (<i>anumana</i>) and testimony (<i>sabda</i> or the word of scriptures).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes1996238_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes1996238-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoni2000367–377_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoni2000367–377-16">[16]</a></sup> These ideas are elaborated in Jain texts such as <i>Tattvarthasūtra</i>, <i>Parvacanasara</i>, <i>Nandi</i> and <i>Anuyogadvarini</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200275–76,_131,_229–230_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200275–76,_131,_229–230-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoni2000367–377_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoni2000367–377-16">[16]</a></sup> Some Jain texts add analogy (<i>upamana</i>) as the fourth reliable means, in a manner similar to <a href="/wiki/Pramana" title="Pramana">epistemological theories</a> found in other Indian religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002229–230_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002229–230-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Jainism, <i>jnāna</i> (knowledge) is said to be of five kinds – <i>mati jñāna</i> (sensory knowledge), <i>śrutu jñāna</i> (scriptural knowledge), <i>avadhi jñāna</i> (<a href="/wiki/Clairvoyance" title="Clairvoyance">clairvoyance</a>), <i>manah prayāya Jñāna</i> (<a href="/wiki/Telepathy" title="Telepathy">telepathy</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Kevala_jnana" title="Kevala jnana">kevala jnana</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">omniscience</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTES.A._Jain199216_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTES.A._Jain199216-19">[19]</a></sup> According to the Jain text <i>Tattvartha sūtra</i>, the first two are indirect knowledge and the remaining three are direct knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain20116_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain20116-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soul_and_karma">Soul and karma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Soul and karma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Karma_in_Jainism" title="Karma in Jainism">Karma in Jainism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jiva.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1142" data-file-width="2042" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Jiva.jpg/220px-Jiva.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Jiva.jpg/330px-Jiva.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Jiva.jpg/440px-Jiva.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jiva.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Classification of <i>Saṃsāri Jīvas</i> (transmigrating souls) in Jainism</div></div></div>
<p>According to Jainism, the existence of "a bound and ever changing soul" is a self-evident truth, an axiom which does not need to be proven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998103_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998103-21">[21]</a></sup> It maintains that there are numerous souls, but every one of them has three qualities (<i><a href="/wiki/Gu%E1%B9%87a" title="Guṇa">Guṇa</a></i>): consciousness (<i>chaitanya</i>, the most important), bliss (<i>sukha</i>) and vibrational energy (<i>virya</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998104–106_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998104–106-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>It further claims the vibration draws karmic particles to the soul and creates bondages, but is also what adds merit or demerit to the soul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998104–106_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998104–106-22">[22]</a></sup> Jain texts state that souls exist as "clothed with material bodies", where it entirely fills up the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925194_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925194-23">[23]</a></sup> Karma, as in other Indian religions, connotes in Jainism the universal cause and effect law. However, it is envisioned as a material substance (subtle matter) that can bind to the soul, travel with the soul in bound form between rebirths, and affect the suffering and happiness experienced by the <i>jiva</i> in the <i>lokas</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong201392–95_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong201392–95-24">[24]</a></sup> Karma is believed to obscure and obstruct the innate nature and striving of the soul, as well as its spiritual potential in the next rebirth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200299–103_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200299–103-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Sa.E1.B9.83s.C4.81ra"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Saṃsāra">Saṃsāra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Saṃsāra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%83s%C4%81ra_(Jainism)" title="Saṃsāra (Jainism)">Saṃsāra (Jainism)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vitalism_(Jainism)" title="Vitalism (Jainism)">Vitalism (Jainism)</a></div>
<p>The conceptual framework of the Saṃsāra doctrine differs between Jainism and other Indian religions. Soul (<i>jiva</i>) is accepted as a truth, as in Hinduism but not Buddhism. The cycle of rebirths has a definite beginning and end in Jainism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1980226_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1980226-26">[26]</a></sup> Jain theosophy asserts that each soul passes through 8,400,000 birth-situations as they circle through <i>Saṃsāra</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1980228_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1980228-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini2000130–131_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini2000130–131-28">[28]</a></sup> going through five types of bodies: earth bodies, water bodies, fire bodies, air bodies and vegetable lives, constantly changing with all human and non-human activities from rainfall to breathing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1980223–225_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1980223–225-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Harming any life form is a sin in Jainism, with negative karmic effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1980224–225_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1980224–225-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESethia200430–31_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESethia200430–31-31">[31]</a></sup> Jainism states that souls begin in a primordial state, and either evolve to a higher state or regress if driven by their karma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1980227–228_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1980227–228-32">[32]</a></sup> It further clarifies that <i>abhavya</i> (incapable) souls can never attain <i><a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a></i> (liberation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1980226_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1980226-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002104–105_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002104–105-33">[33]</a></sup> It explains that the <i>abhavya</i> state is entered after an intentional and shockingly evil act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1980225_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1980225-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Souls can be good or evil in Jainism, unlike the <a href="/wiki/Nondualism" title="Nondualism">nondualism</a> of some forms of Hinduism and Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002104–105_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002104–105-33">[33]</a></sup> According to Jainism, a <i>Siddha</i> (liberated soul) has gone beyond <i>Saṃsāra</i>, is at the apex, is omniscient, and remains there eternally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1980222–223_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1980222–223-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmology">Cosmology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cosmology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jain_cosmology" title="Jain cosmology">Jain cosmology</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:322px;max-width:322px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:164px;max-width:164px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:111px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jain_universe.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="366" data-file-width="533" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Jain_universe.JPG/162px-Jain_universe.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Jain_universe.JPG/243px-Jain_universe.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Jain_universe.JPG/324px-Jain_universe.JPG 2x" width="162"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Rebirth <i>loka</i> (realms of existence) in Jain cosmology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200290–92_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200290–92-36">[36]</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:154px;max-width:154px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:111px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jain_Cosmic_Time_Cycle.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="584" data-file-width="798" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Jain_Cosmic_Time_Cycle.jpg/152px-Jain_Cosmic_Time_Cycle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Jain_Cosmic_Time_Cycle.jpg/228px-Jain_Cosmic_Time_Cycle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Jain_Cosmic_Time_Cycle.jpg/304px-Jain_Cosmic_Time_Cycle.jpg 2x" width="152"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Division of time in Jain cosmology.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Jain texts propound that the universe consists of many eternal <i>lokas</i> (realms of existence). As in Buddhism and Hinduism, both time and the universe are eternal, but the universe is transient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925241_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925241-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong201383–85_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong201383–85-38">[38]</a></sup> The universe, body, matter and time are considered separate from the soul (<i>jiva</i>). Their interaction explains life, living, death and rebirth in Jain philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong201383–85_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong201383–85-38">[38]</a></sup> The Jain cosmic universe has three parts, the upper, middle, and lower worlds (<i>urdhva loka</i>, <i>madhya loka</i>, and <i>adho loka</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah199825_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah199825-39">[39]</a></sup> Jainism states that <i>Kāla</i> (time) is without beginning and eternal;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoniger1999551_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoniger1999551-40">[40]</a></sup> the cosmic wheel of time, <i>kālachakra</i>, rotates ceaselessly. In this part of the universe, it explains, there are six periods of time within two eons (<i>ara</i>), and in the first eon the universe generates, and in the next it degenerates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain201146_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain201146-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thus, it divides the worldly cycle of time into two half-cycles, <i>utsarpiṇī</i> (ascending, progressive prosperity and happiness) and <i><a href="/wiki/Avasarpi%E1%B9%87%C4%AB" title="Avasarpiṇī">avasarpiṇī</a></i> (descending, increasing sorrow and immorality).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoniger1999551_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoniger1999551-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpinder_Singh2016313_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpinder_Singh2016313-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925271–272_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925271–272-43">[43]</a></sup> It states that the world is currently in the fifth <i>ara</i> of <i>avasarpiṇī</i>, full of sorrow and religious decline, where the height of living beings shrinks. According to Jainism, after the sixth <i>ara</i>, the universe will be reawakened in a new cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200213_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200213-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChampat_Rai_Jain1929b124_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChampat_Rai_Jain1929b124-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a27_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a27-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="God">God</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: God">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jain_24-Tirthankaras.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="805" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Jain_24-Tirthankaras.jpg/220px-Jain_24-Tirthankaras.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Jain_24-Tirthankaras.jpg/330px-Jain_24-Tirthankaras.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Jain_24-Tirthankaras.jpg/440px-Jain_24-Tirthankaras.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jain_24-Tirthankaras.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jain miniature painting of 24 tirthankaras, <a href="/wiki/Jaipur" title="Jaipur">Jaipur</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1850</span></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/God_in_Jainism" title="God in Jainism">God in Jainism</a></div>
<p>Jainism is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transtheistic" title="Transtheistic">transtheistic</a> religion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZimmer1953182_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZimmer1953182-47">[47]</a></sup> holding that the <a href="/wiki/Jainism_and_non-creationism" title="Jainism and non-creationism">universe was not created</a>, and will exist forever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925241_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925241-37">[37]</a></sup> It is independent, having no creator, governor, judge, or destroyer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong201383–85_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong201383–85-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925241–242_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925241–242-48">[48]</a></sup> In this, it is unlike the Abrahamic religions and the theistic strands of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, but similar to Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925241–243_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925241–243-49">[49]</a></sup> However, Jainism believes in the world of heavenly and hell beings who are born, die and are reborn like earthly beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925247–249,_262–263_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925247–249,_262–263-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200220–21,_34–35,_74,_91,_95–96,_103_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200220–21,_34–35,_74,_91,_95–96,_103-51">[51]</a></sup> The souls who live happily in the body of a heavenly celestial do so because of their positive karma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925262–263_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925262–263-52">[52]</a></sup> It is further stated that they possess a more transcendent knowledge about material things and can anticipate events in the human realms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925262–263_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925262–263-52">[52]</a></sup> However, once their past karmic merit is exhausted, it is explained that their souls are reborn again as humans, animals or other beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925262–263_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925262–263-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200291,_95–96_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200291,_95–96-53">[53]</a></sup> The perfect enlightened souls <i>with</i> a body are called <i><a href="/wiki/Arihant_(Jainism)" title="Arihant (Jainism)">Arihants</a></i> (victors) and perfect souls <i>without</i> a body are called <i><a href="/wiki/Siddha" title="Siddha">Siddhas</a></i> (liberated souls). Only a soul with human body can attain enlightenment and liberation. The liberated beings are the supreme beings and are worshipped by all heavenly, earthly and hellish beings who aspire to attain liberation themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1980222–223_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1980222–223-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZimmer1953182_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZimmer1953182-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERankinMardia201340_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERankinMardia201340-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Salvation.2C_liberation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Salvation,_liberation">Salvation, liberation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Salvation, liberation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Moksha_(Jainism)" title="Moksha (Jainism)">Moksha (Jainism)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ratnatraya" title="Ratnatraya">Ratnatraya</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gunasthana" title="Gunasthana">Gunasthana</a></div>
<p>Purification of soul and liberation can be achieved through the path of four jewels:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain20116_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain20116-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2001a6–7_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2001a6–7-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFohr20159–10,_37_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFohr20159–10,_37-56">[56]</a></sup> <i>Samyak Darśana</i> (Correct View), meaning faith, acceptance of the truth of soul (<i>jīva</i>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998141–147_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998141–147-57">[57]</a></sup> <i>Samyak Gyana</i> (Correct Knowledge), meaning undoubting knowledge of the <i>tattvas</i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998148,_200_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998148,_200-58">[58]</a></sup> and <i>Samyak Charitra</i> (Correct Conduct), meaning behavior consistent with the Five vows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998148,_200_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998148,_200-58">[58]</a></sup> Jain texts often add <i>samyak tap</i> (Correct Asceticism) as a fourth jewel, emphasizing belief in ascetic practices as the means to liberation (<a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2001a7_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2001a7-59">[59]</a></sup> The four jewels are called <i>Moksha Marg</i> (the path of liberation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2001a6–7_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2001a6–7-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_principles">Main principles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Main principles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Non-violence_.28ahimsa.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-violence_(ahimsa)">Non-violence (<i>ahimsa</i>)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Non-violence (ahimsa)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="ahiṃsā"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ahimsa_in_Jainism" title="Ahimsa in Jainism">Ahimsa in Jainism</a></div>
<p>The principle of <i><a href="/wiki/Ahimsa_in_Jainism" title="Ahimsa in Jainism">ahimsa</a></i> (non-violence or non-injury) is a fundamental tenet of Jainism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160-60">[60]</a></sup> It holds that one must abandon all violent activity and that without such a commitment to non-violence all religious behavior is worthless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160-60">[60]</a></sup> In Jain theology, it does not matter how correct or defensible the violence may be, one must not kill or harm any being, and non-violence is the highest religious duty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarkhamLohr200971_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkhamLohr200971-61">[61]</a></sup> Jain texts such as <i><a href="/wiki/Acharanga_Sutra" title="Acharanga Sutra">Acaranga Sūtra</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tattvartha_Sutra" title="Tattvartha Sutra">Tattvarthasūtra</a></i> state that one must renounce all killing of living beings, whether tiny or large, movable or immovable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrice201090_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrice201090-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160–162_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160–162-63">[63]</a></sup> Its theology teaches that one must neither kill another living being, nor cause another to kill, nor consent to any killing directly or indirectly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarkhamLohr200971_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkhamLohr200971-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrice201090_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrice201090-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, Jainism emphasizes non-violence against all beings not only in action but also in speech and in thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrice201090_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrice201090-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160–162_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160–162-63">[63]</a></sup> It states that instead of hate or violence against anyone, "all living creatures must help each other".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160–162_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160–162-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[a]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jains believe that violence negatively affects and destroys one's soul, particularly when the violence is done with intent, hate or carelessness, or when one indirectly causes or consents to the killing of a human or non-human living being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160–162_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160–162-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The doctrine exists in Hinduism and Buddhism, but is most highly developed in Jainism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002160-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESundararajanMukherji1997392–417_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESundararajanMukherji1997392–417-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIzawa200878–81_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIzawa200878–81-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESethia20042_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESethia20042-68">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinternitz1993409_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinternitz1993409-69">[68]</a></sup> The theological basis of non-violence as the highest religious duty has been interpreted by some Jain scholars not to "be driven by merit from giving or compassion to other creatures, nor a duty to rescue all creatures", but resulting from "continual self-discipline", a cleansing of the soul that leads to one's own spiritual development which ultimately affects one's salvation and release from rebirths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200288–89,_257–258_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200288–89,_257–258-70">[69]</a></sup> Jains believe that causing injury to any being in any form creates bad <a href="/wiki/Karma_in_Jainism" title="Karma in Jainism">karma</a> which affects one's rebirth, future well-being and causes suffering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2008892–894_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2008892–894-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGranoff1992_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGranoff1992-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Late medieval Jain scholars re-examined the <i>Ahiṃsā</i> doctrine when faced with external threat or violence. For example, they justified violence by monks to protect nuns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002162–163_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002162–163-73">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELorenzen197861–75_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELorenzen197861–75-74">[73]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Paul_Dundas" title="Paul Dundas">Dundas</a>, the Jain scholar <a href="/wiki/Jinadattasuri" title="Jinadattasuri">Jinadattasuri</a> wrote during a time of Muslim destruction of temples and persecution that "anybody engaged in a religious activity who was forced to fight and kill somebody would not lose any spiritual merit but instead attain deliverance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002163_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002163-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, examples in Jain texts that condone fighting and killing under certain circumstances are relatively rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002162–163_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002162–163-73">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[b]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Many-sided_reality_.28anek.C4.81ntav.C4.81da.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Many-sided_reality_(anekāntavāda)">Many-sided reality (<i>anekāntavāda</i>)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Many-sided reality (anekāntavāda)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anekantavada" title="Anekantavada">Anekantavada</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1808" data-file-width="2463" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg/220px-Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg/330px-Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg/440px-Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Jain_temple_Anekantavada_doctrine_artwork.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jain temple painting explaining Anekantavada with <a href="/wiki/Blind_men_and_an_elephant" title="Blind men and an elephant">Blind men and an elephant</a></div></div></div>
<p>The second main principle of Jainism is <i>anekāntavāda</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECharitrapragya200475–79_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECharitrapragya200475–79-78">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002229–231_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002229–231-79">[77]</a></sup> from <i>anekānta</i> ("many-sidedness") and <i>vada</i> ("doctrine").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECharitrapragya200475–79_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECharitrapragya200475–79-78">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002229–231_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002229–231-79">[77]</a></sup> The doctrine states that truth and reality are complex and always have multiple aspects. It further states that reality can be experienced, but cannot be fully expressed with language. It suggests that human attempts to communicate are <i>Naya</i>, "partial expression of the truth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECharitrapragya200475–79_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECharitrapragya200475–79-78">[76]</a></sup> According to it, one can experience the taste of truth, but cannot fully express that taste through language. It holds that attempts to express experience are <i>syāt</i>, or valid "in some respect", but remain "perhaps, just one perspective, incomplete".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup> It concludes that in the same way, spiritual truths can be experienced but not fully expressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECharitrapragya200475–79_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECharitrapragya200475–79-78">[76]</a></sup> It suggests that the great error is belief in <i>ekānta</i> (one-sidedness), where some relative truth is treated as absolute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchwartz2018_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchwartz2018-81">[79]</a></sup> The doctrine is ancient, found in Buddhist texts such as the <i>Samaññaphala Sutta</i>. The Jain Agamas suggest that Mahāvīra's approach to answering all metaphysical philosophical questions was a "qualified yes" (<i>syāt</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatilal1990301–305_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatilal1990301–305-82">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBalcerowicz2015205–218_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBalcerowicz2015205–218-83">[81]</a></sup> These texts identify <i>anekāntavāda</i> as a key difference from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a>'s teachings. The Buddha taught the Middle Way, rejecting extremes of the answer "it is" or "it is not" to metaphysical questions. The Mahāvīra, in contrast, taught his followers to accept both "it is", and "it is not", qualified with "perhaps", to understand Absolute Reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatilal1998128–135_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatilal1998128–135-84">[82]</a></sup> The permanent being is conceptualized as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jiva_(Jainism)" title="Jiva (Jainism)">jiva</a></i> (soul) and <i><a href="/wiki/Ajiva" title="Ajiva">ajiva</a></i> (matter) within a dualistic <i>anekāntavāda</i> framework.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200290–99,_104–105,_229–233_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200290–99,_104–105,_229–233-85">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Paul Dundas, in contemporary times the <i>anekāntavāda</i> doctrine has been interpreted by some Jains as intending to "promote a universal religious tolerance", and a teaching of "plurality" and "benign attitude to other [ethical, religious] positions". Dundas states this is a misreading of historical texts and Mahāvīra's teachings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002232–234_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002232–234-86">[84]</a></sup> According to him, the "many pointedness, multiple perspective" teachings of the Mahāvīra is about the nature of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_(philosophy)" title="Absolute (philosophy)">absolute reality</a> and human existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESethia200486–91_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESethia200486–91-87">[85]</a></sup> He claims that it is not about condoning activities such as killing animals for food, nor violence against disbelievers or any other living being as "perhaps right".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002232–234_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002232–234-86">[84]</a></sup> The five vows for Jain monks and nuns, for example, are strict requirements and there is no "perhaps" about them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong200998–106_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong200998–106-88">[86]</a></sup> Similarly, since ancient times, Jainism co-existed with Buddhism and Hinduism according to Dundas, but Jainism disagreed, in specific areas, with the knowledge systems and beliefs of these traditions, and vice versa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002233_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002233-89">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Non-attachment_.28aparigraha.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-attachment_(aparigraha)">Non-attachment (<i>aparigraha</i>)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Non-attachment (aparigraha)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="aparigraha"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aparigraha" title="Aparigraha">Aparigraha</a></div>
<p>The third main principle in Jainism is <i>aparigraha</i> which means non-attachment to worldly possessions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah2004112_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah2004112-90">[88]</a></sup> For monks and nuns, Jainism requires a vow of complete non-possession of any property, relations and emotions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002117,_152_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002117,_152-91">[89]</a></sup> The ascetic is a wandering mendicant in the Digambara tradition, or a resident mendicant in the <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Av%C4%93t%C4%81mbara" title="Śvētāmbara">Śvētāmbara</a> tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002117,_152_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002117,_152-91">[89]</a></sup> For Jain laypersons, it recommends limited possession of property that has been honestly earned, and giving excess property to charity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah2004112_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah2004112-90">[88]</a></sup> According to Natubhai Shah, <i>aparigraha</i> applies to both the material and the psychic. Material possessions refer to various forms of property. Psychic possessions refer to emotions, likes and dislikes, and attachments of any form. Unchecked attachment to possessions is said to result in direct harm to one's personality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah2004112–113_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah2004112–113-92">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jain_ethics_and_five_vows">Jain ethics and five vows</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Jain ethics and five vows">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethics_of_Jainism" title="Ethics of Jainism">Ethics of Jainism</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Yamas#Five_Yamas" title="Yamas">Yamas § Five Yamas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nishidhi_stone_with_14th_century_Old_Kannada_inscription_from_Tavanandi_forest.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5184" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Nishidhi_stone_with_14th_century_Old_Kannada_inscription_from_Tavanandi_forest.JPG/170px-Nishidhi_stone_with_14th_century_Old_Kannada_inscription_from_Tavanandi_forest.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Nishidhi_stone_with_14th_century_Old_Kannada_inscription_from_Tavanandi_forest.JPG/255px-Nishidhi_stone_with_14th_century_Old_Kannada_inscription_from_Tavanandi_forest.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Nishidhi_stone_with_14th_century_Old_Kannada_inscription_from_Tavanandi_forest.JPG/340px-Nishidhi_stone_with_14th_century_Old_Kannada_inscription_from_Tavanandi_forest.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nishidhi_stone_with_14th_century_Old_Kannada_inscription_from_Tavanandi_forest.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Doddahundi_nishidhi_inscription" title="Doddahundi nishidhi inscription">Nishidhi stone</a>, depicting the vow of <i>sallekhana</i>, 14th century, <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a></div></div></div>
<p>Jainism teaches five ethical duties, which it calls five vows. These are called <i>anuvratas</i> (small vows) for Jain laypersons, and <i>mahavratas</i> (great vows) for Jain mendicants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228–231_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228–231-93">[91]</a></sup> For both, its moral precepts preface that the Jain has access to a <i><a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">guru</a></i> (teacher, counsellor), <i>deva</i> (Jina, god), doctrine, and that the individual is free from five offences: doubts about the faith, indecisiveness about the truths of Jainism, sincere desire for Jain teachings, recognition of fellow Jains, and admiration for their spiritual pursuits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228-94">[92]</a></sup> Such a person undertakes the following Five vows of Jainism:
</p>
<ol><li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahi%E1%B9%83s%C4%81" title="Ahiṃsā">Ahiṃsā</a></i>, "intentional non-violence" or "noninjury":<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228-94">[92]</a></sup> The first major vow taken by Jains is to cause no harm to other human beings, as well as all living beings (particularly animals).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228-94">[92]</a></sup> This is the highest ethical duty in Jainism, and it applies not only to one's actions, but demands that one be non-violent in one's speech and thoughts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pkshah5v_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-pkshah5v-95">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain201233_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain201233-96">[94]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Satya" title="Satya">Satya</a></i>, "truth": This vow is to always speak the truth. Neither lie, nor speak what is not true, and do not encourage others or approve anyone who speaks an untruth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228–231_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228–231-93">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pkshah5v_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-pkshah5v-95">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteya" title="Asteya">Asteya</a></i>, "not stealing": A Jain layperson should not take anything that is not willingly given.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228-94">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain201268_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain201268-97">[95]</a></sup> Additionally, a Jain mendicant should ask for permission to take it if something is being given.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925231_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925231-98">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Brahmacharya" title="Brahmacharya">Brahmacharya</a></i>, "celibacy": Abstinence from sex and sensual pleasures is prescribed for Jain monks and nuns. For laypersons, the vow means chastity, faithfulness to one's partner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228–231_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228–231-93">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pkshah5v_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-pkshah5v-95">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aparigraha" title="Aparigraha">Aparigraha</a></i>, "non-possessiveness": This includes non-attachment to material and psychological possessions, avoiding craving and greed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228–231_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925228–231-93">[91]</a></sup> Jain monks and nuns completely renounce property and social relations, own nothing and are attached to no one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah2004112_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah2004112-90">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong2009109_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong2009109-99">[97]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Jainism prescribes seven supplementary vows, including three <i>guņa vratas</i> (merit vows) and four <i>śikşā vratas</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain201287–88_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain201287–88-100">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETukol19765_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETukol19765-101">[99]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Sallekhana" title="Sallekhana">Sallekhana</a></i> (or <i>Santhara</i>) vow is a "religious death" ritual observed at the end of life, historically by Jain monks and nuns, but rare in the modern age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002179–180_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002179–180-102">[100]</a></sup> In this vow, there is voluntary and gradual reduction of food and liquid intake to end one's life by choice and with dispassion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini200016_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini200016-103">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETukol19767_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETukol19767-104">[102]</a></sup> This is believed to reduce negative karma that affects a soul's future rebirths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams1991166–167_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams1991166–167-105">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Practices">Practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asceticism_and_monasticism">Asceticism and monasticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Asceticism and monasticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">Asceticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jain_monasticism" title="Jain monasticism">Jain monasticism</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:268px;max-width:268px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:76px;max-width:76px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:110px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ellora,_cave_33,_Digambar_Jain_guru_(9841591645).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="5184" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Ellora%2C_cave_33%2C_Digambar_Jain_guru_%289841591645%29.jpg/74px-Ellora%2C_cave_33%2C_Digambar_Jain_guru_%289841591645%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Ellora%2C_cave_33%2C_Digambar_Jain_guru_%289841591645%29.jpg/111px-Ellora%2C_cave_33%2C_Digambar_Jain_guru_%289841591645%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Ellora%2C_cave_33%2C_Digambar_Jain_guru_%289841591645%29.jpg/148px-Ellora%2C_cave_33%2C_Digambar_Jain_guru_%289841591645%29.jpg 2x" width="74"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Digambara monk</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:97px;max-width:97px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:110px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acharya_Vijayavallabhasuri.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1746" data-file-width="1501" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Acharya_Vijayavallabhasuri.jpg/95px-Acharya_Vijayavallabhasuri.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Acharya_Vijayavallabhasuri.jpg/143px-Acharya_Vijayavallabhasuri.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Acharya_Vijayavallabhasuri.jpg/190px-Acharya_Vijayavallabhasuri.jpg 2x" width="95"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Śvētāmbara-Deravasi monk</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:89px;max-width:89px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:110px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jain_Sthanakvasi_monk.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="756" data-file-width="594" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Jain_Sthanakvasi_monk.jpg/87px-Jain_Sthanakvasi_monk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Jain_Sthanakvasi_monk.jpg/131px-Jain_Sthanakvasi_monk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Jain_Sthanakvasi_monk.jpg/174px-Jain_Sthanakvasi_monk.jpg 2x" width="87"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Śvētāmbara-Sthanakwasi monk</div></div></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:267px;max-width:267px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:106px;max-width:106px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:183px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jain_1.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="341" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Jain_1.jpg/104px-Jain_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Jain_1.jpg/156px-Jain_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Jain_1.jpg/208px-Jain_1.jpg 2x" width="104"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A Śvētāmbara nun (early 20th-century)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:157px;max-width:157px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:183px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Viramati_Mataji.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2314" data-file-width="1945" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Viramati_Mataji.jpg/155px-Viramati_Mataji.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Viramati_Mataji.jpg/233px-Viramati_Mataji.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Viramati_Mataji.jpg/310px-Viramati_Mataji.jpg 2x" width="155"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A Digambara nun</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Of the major Indian religions, Jainism has had the strongest ascetic tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2001a118–122_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2001a118–122-106">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQvarnström2003113_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQvarnström2003113-107">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQvarnström2003169–174,_178–198_with_footnotes_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQvarnström2003169–174,_178–198_with_footnotes-108">[106]</a></sup> Ascetic life may include nakedness, symbolizing non-possession even of clothes, fasting, body mortification, and penance, to burn away past karma and stop producing new karma, both of which are believed essential for reaching <i>siddha</i> and <i>moksha</i> ("liberation from rebirths" and "salvation").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2001a118–122_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2001a118–122-106">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQvarnström2003205–212_with_footnotes_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQvarnström2003205–212_with_footnotes-109">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBalcerowicz2015144–150_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBalcerowicz2015144–150-110">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jain texts like <i>Tattvartha Sūtra</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Uttaradhyayana" title="Uttaradhyayana">Uttaradhyayana Sūtra</a></i> discuss austerities in detail. Six outer and six inner practices are oft-repeated in later Jain texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2001a120–21_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2001a120–21-111">[109]</a></sup> Outer austerities include complete fasting, eating limited amounts, eating restricted items, abstaining from tasty foods, mortifying the flesh, and guarding the flesh (avoiding anything that is a source of temptation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2001a120–122_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2001a120–122-112">[110]</a></sup> Inner austerities include expiation, confession, respecting and assisting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mendicants" title="Mendicants">mendicants</a>, studying, meditation, and ignoring bodily wants in order to abandon the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2001a120–122_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2001a120–122-112">[110]</a></sup> Lists of internal and external austerities vary with the text and tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQvarnström2003182_with_footnote_3_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQvarnström2003182_with_footnote_3-113">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson1995196–197_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson1995196–197-114">[112]</a></sup> Asceticism is viewed as a means to control desires, and to purify the <i>jiva</i> (soul).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQvarnström2003169–174,_178–198_with_footnotes_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQvarnström2003169–174,_178–198_with_footnotes-108">[106]</a></sup> The <i>tirthankaras</i> such as the Mahāvīra (Vardhamana) set an example by performing severe austerities for twelve years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2001a121–122_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2001a121–122-115">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShanti_Lal_Jain199851_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShanti_Lal_Jain199851-116">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBalcerowicz201515–18,_41–43_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBalcerowicz201515–18,_41–43-117">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Monastic organization, <i>sangh</i>, has a four-fold order consisting of <i>sadhu</i> (male ascetics, <i>muni</i>), <i>sadhvi</i> (female ascetics, <i>aryika</i>), <i><a href="/wiki/%C5%9Ar%C4%81vaka_(Jainism)" title="Śrāvaka (Jainism)">śrāvaka</a></i> (laymen), and <i>śrāvikā</i> (laywomen). The latter two support the ascetics and their monastic organizations called <i>gacch</i> or <i>samuday</i>, in autonomous regional Jain congregations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2001a48–49_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2001a48–49-118">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBalcerowicz200917_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBalcerowicz200917-119">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah20042–3_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah20042–3-120">[118]</a></sup> Jain monastic rules have encouraged the use of mouth cover, as well as the <i>Dandasan</i> – a long stick with woolen threads – to gently remove ants and insects that may come in their path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain2013197_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain2013197-121">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002152,_163–164_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002152,_163–164-122">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998190_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998190-123">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_and_fasting">Food and fasting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Food and fasting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Jain_vegetarianism" title="Jain vegetarianism">Jain vegetarianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fasting_in_Jainism" title="Fasting in Jainism">Fasting in Jainism</a></div>
<p>The practice of non-violence towards all living beings has led to Jain culture being <a href="/wiki/Jain_vegetarianism" title="Jain vegetarianism">vegetarian</a>. Devout Jains practice <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lacto-vegetarianism" title="Lacto-vegetarianism">lacto-vegetarianism</a>, meaning that they eat no eggs, but accept dairy products if there is no violence against animals during their production. <a href="/wiki/Veganism" title="Veganism">Veganism</a> is encouraged if there are concerns about animal welfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVoorst2015105_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVoorst2015105-124">[122]</a></sup> Jain monks, nuns and some followers avoid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Root_vegetables" title="Root vegetables">root vegetables</a> such as potatoes, onions, and garlic because tiny organisms are injured when the plant is pulled up, and because a bulb or tuber's ability to sprout is seen as characteristic of a higher living being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESangave1980260_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESangave1980260-125">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[c]</a></sup> Jain monks and advanced laypeople avoid eating after sunset, observing a vow of <i>ratri-bhojana-tyaga-vrata</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini2000285_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini2000285-127">[124]</a></sup> Monks observe a stricter vow by eating only once a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini2000285_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini2000285-127">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jains fast particularly during festivals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley200985_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley200985-128">[125]</a></sup> This practice is called <i>upavasa</i>, <i>tapasya</i> or <i>vrata</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley200985–86_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley200985–86-129">[126]</a></sup> and may be practiced according to one's ability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERam_Bhushan_Prasad_Singh200892–94_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERam_Bhushan_Prasad_Singh200892–94-130">[127]</a></sup> Digambaras fast for <i>Dasa-laksana-parvan</i>, eating only one or two meals per day, drinking only boiled water for ten days, or fasting completely on the first and last days of the festival,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley200972_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley200972-131">[128]</a></sup> mimicking the practices of a Jain mendicant for the period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley200972_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley200972-131">[128]</a></sup> Śvētāmbara Jains do similarly in the eight day <i>paryusana</i> with <i>samvatsari-pratikramana</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley200972,_85–86_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley200972,_85–86-132">[129]</a></sup> The practice is believed to remove karma from one's soul and provides merit (<i>punya</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley200985_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley200985-128">[125]</a></sup> A "one day" fast lasts about 36 hours, starting at sunset before the day of the fast and ending 48 minutes after sunrise the day after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley200985_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley200985-128">[125]</a></sup> Among laypeople, fasting is more commonly observed by women, as it shows her piety and religious purity, gains merit earning and helps ensure future well-being for her family. Some religious fasts are observed in a social and supportive female group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley200986_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley200986-133">[130]</a></sup> Long fasts are celebrated by friends and families with special ceremonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley200986_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley200986-133">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meditation">Meditation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Meditation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jain_meditation" title="Jain meditation">Jain meditation</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:322px;max-width:322px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:168px;max-width:168px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:111px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jain_meditation.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="471" data-file-width="704" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Jain_meditation.jpg/166px-Jain_meditation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Jain_meditation.jpg/249px-Jain_meditation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Jain_meditation.jpg/332px-Jain_meditation.jpg 2x" width="166"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:150px;max-width:150px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:111px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3376" data-file-width="4503" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg/148px-Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg/222px-Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg/296px-Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg 2x" width="148"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Left: Jain nuns meditating, Right: 10th-century <a href="/wiki/Gommateshwara_statue" title="Gommateshwara statue">Gommateshwara statue</a> depicting standing meditation (<a href="/wiki/Kayotsarga" title="Kayotsarga">Kayotsarga</a> posture)</div></div></div></div>
<p>Jainism considers meditation (<i>dhyana</i>) a necessary practice, but its goals are very different from those in Buddhism and Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002166–169_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002166–169-134">[131]</a></sup> In Jainism, meditation is concerned more with stopping karmic attachments and activity, not as a means to transformational insights or self-realization in other Indian religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002166–169_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002166–169-134">[131]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Padmanabh_Jaini" title="Padmanabh Jaini">Padmanabh Jaini</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/S%C4%81m%C4%81yika" title="Sāmāyika">Sāmāyika</a></i> is a practice of "brief periods in meditation" in Jainism that is a part of <i>siksavrata</i> (ritual restraint).<sup class="reference"
