id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998180–181_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998180–181-135">[132]</a></sup> The goal of <i>Sāmāyika</i> is to achieve equanimity, and it is the second <i>siksavrata</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[d]</a></sup> The <i>samayika</i> ritual is practiced at least three times a day by mendicants, while a layperson includes it with other ritual practices such as <i>Puja</i> in a Jain temple and doing charity work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998180–182_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998180–182-137">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTES.A._Jain1992261_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTES.A._Jain1992261-138">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah2004128–131_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah2004128–131-139">[135]</a></sup> According to Johnson, as well as Jaini, <i>samayika</i> connotes more than meditation, and for a Jain householder is the voluntary ritual practice of "assuming temporary ascetic status".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson1995189–190_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson1995189–190-140">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[e]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rituals_and_worship">Rituals and worship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Rituals and worship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jain_rituals" title="Jain rituals">Jain rituals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shravanbelgola_Gomateshvara_feet_prayer1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Shravanbelgola_Gomateshvara_feet_prayer1.jpg/170px-Shravanbelgola_Gomateshvara_feet_prayer1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Shravanbelgola_Gomateshvara_feet_prayer1.jpg/255px-Shravanbelgola_Gomateshvara_feet_prayer1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Shravanbelgola_Gomateshvara_feet_prayer1.jpg/340px-Shravanbelgola_Gomateshvara_feet_prayer1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shravanbelgola_Gomateshvara_feet_prayer1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Praying at the feet of a statue of <a href="/wiki/Bahubali" title="Bahubali">Bahubali</a></div></div></div>
<p>There are many rituals in Jainism's various sects. According to Dundas, the ritualistic lay path among Śvētāmbara Jains is "heavily imbued with ascetic values", where the rituals either revere or celebrate the ascetic life of Tirthankaras, or progressively approach the psychological and physical life of an ascetic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002187–189_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002187–189-143">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998162–165,_295–296_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998162–165,_295–296-144">[139]</a></sup> The ultimate ritual is <i>sallekhana</i>, a religious death through ascetic abandonment of food and drinks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002187–189_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002187–189-143">[138]</a></sup> The Digambara Jains follow the same theme, but the life cycle and religious rituals are closer to a Hindu liturgy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002187–189_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002187–189-143">[138]</a></sup> The overlap is mainly in the life cycle (rites-of-passage) rituals, and likely developed because Jain and Hindu societies overlapped, and rituals were viewed as necessary and secular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998291–299_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998291–299-145">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley2009186–187_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley2009186–187-146">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jains ritually worship numerous deities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998162–165,_295–296_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998162–165,_295–296-144">[139]</a></sup> especially the <i><a href="/wiki/Arihant_(Jainism)" title="Arihant (Jainism)">Jinas</a></i>. In Jainism a <i>Jina</i> as <i>deva</i> is not an <a href="/wiki/Avatar" title="Avatar">avatar</a> (incarnation), but the highest state of omniscience that an ascetic <i>tirthankara</i> achieved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998295–299_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998295–299-147">[142]</a></sup> Out of the 24 Tirthankaras, Jains predominantly worship four: Mahāvīra, <a href="/wiki/Parshvanatha" title="Parshvanatha">Parshvanatha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neminatha" title="Neminatha">Neminatha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rishabhanatha" title="Rishabhanatha">Rishabhanatha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200240_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200240-148">[143]</a></sup> Among the non-<i>tirthankara</i> saints, devotional worship is common for <a href="/wiki/Bahubali" title="Bahubali">Bahubali</a> among the Digambaras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2010182–184_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2010182–184-149">[144]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Panch_Kalyanaka" title="Panch Kalyanaka">Panch Kalyanaka</a></i> rituals remember the five life events of the <i>tirthankaras</i>, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Panch_Kalyanaka_Pratishtha_Mahotsava" title="Panch Kalyanaka Pratishtha Mahotsava">Panch Kalyanaka Pratishtha Mahotsava</a></i>, <i>Panch Kalyanaka Puja</i> and <i>Snatrapuja</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998196,_343,_347_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998196,_343,_347-150">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998196–199_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998196–199-151">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_Jain_ritual_offerings_and_puja_recital_at_a_temple,_worship_in_Jainism.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4256" data-file-width="2832" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/A_Jain_ritual_offerings_and_puja_recital_at_a_temple%2C_worship_in_Jainism.jpg/170px-A_Jain_ritual_offerings_and_puja_recital_at_a_temple%2C_worship_in_Jainism.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/A_Jain_ritual_offerings_and_puja_recital_at_a_temple%2C_worship_in_Jainism.jpg/255px-A_Jain_ritual_offerings_and_puja_recital_at_a_temple%2C_worship_in_Jainism.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/A_Jain_ritual_offerings_and_puja_recital_at_a_temple%2C_worship_in_Jainism.jpg/340px-A_Jain_ritual_offerings_and_puja_recital_at_a_temple%2C_worship_in_Jainism.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_Jain_ritual_offerings_and_puja_recital_at_a_temple,_worship_in_Jainism.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jain worship may include ritual offerings and recitals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley200945–46,_215_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley200945–46,_215-152">[147]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The basic ritual is <i>darsana</i> (seeing) of <i>deva</i>, which includes Jina,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindsay_Jones20054771_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindsay_Jones20054771-153">[148]</a></sup> or other <a href="/wiki/Yaksha" title="Yaksha">yaksas</a>, gods and goddesses such as Brahmadeva, 52 Viras, <a href="/wiki/Padmavati_(Jainism)" title="Padmavati (Jainism)">Padmavati</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ambika_(Jainism)" title="Ambika (Jainism)">Ambika</a> and 16 Vidyadevis (including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarasvati" title="Sarasvati">Sarasvati</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley200933,_59,_92,_138,_191_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley200933,_59,_92,_138,_191-154">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort1987235–255_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort1987235–255-155">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMishraRay2016141–148_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMishraRay2016141–148-156">[151]</a></sup> Terapanthi Digambaras limit their ritual worship to Tirthankaras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a365_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a365-157">[152]</a></sup> The worship ritual is called <i>devapuja</i>, and is found in all Jain sub-traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998199–200_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998199–200-158">[153]</a></sup> Typically, the Jain layperson enters the Derasar (Jain temple) inner sanctum in simple clothing and bare feet with a plate filled with offerings, bows down, says the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namaskar" title="Namaskar">namaskar</a>, completes his or her litany and prayers, sometimes is assisted by the temple priest, leaves the offerings and then departs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998199–200_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998199–200-158">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jain practices include performing <i><a href="/wiki/Abhisheka" title="Abhisheka">abhisheka</a></i> (ceremonial bath) of the images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPratapaditya_Pal198629_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPratapaditya_Pal198629-159">[154]</a></sup> Some Jain sects employ a <i>pujari</i> (also called <i>upadhye</i>), who may be a Hindu, to perform priestly duties at the temple.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002204–205_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002204–205-160">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESalvadori1989169–170_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESalvadori1989169–170-161">[156]</a></sup> More elaborate worship includes offerings such as rice, fresh and dry fruits, flowers, coconut, sweets, and money. Some may light up a lamp with camphor and make auspicious marks with sandalwood paste. Devotees also recite Jain texts, particularly the life stories of the tirthankaras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBabb199632–33_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBabb199632–33-162">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley200945–46,_215_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley200945–46,_215-152">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditional Jains, like Buddhists and Hindus, believe in the efficacy of <a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantras</a> and that certain sounds and words are inherently auspicious, powerful and spiritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200281–82_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200281–82-163">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENayanar200535_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENayanar200535-164">[159]</a></sup> The most famous of the mantras, broadly accepted in various sects of Jainism, is the "five homage" (<i><a href="/wiki/Namokar_Mantra" title="Namokar Mantra">panca namaskara</a></i>) mantra which is believed to be eternal and existent since the first tirthankara's time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200281–82_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200281–82-163">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVoorst2015107_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVoorst2015107-165">[160]</a></sup> Medieval worship practices included making tantric diagrams of the <i>Rishi-mandala</i> including the <i>tirthankaras</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGough20121–47_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGough20121–47-166">[161]</a></sup> The Jain tantric traditions use mantra and rituals that are believed to accrue merit for rebirth realms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2001b417–419_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2001b417–419-167">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals">Festivals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Festivals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jain_festivals" title="Jain festivals">Jain festivals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Das_Lakshana_(Paryusana)_celebrations,_New_York_City_Jain_temple_2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1936" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Das_Lakshana_%28Paryusana%29_celebrations%2C_New_York_City_Jain_temple_2.JPG/170px-Das_Lakshana_%28Paryusana%29_celebrations%2C_New_York_City_Jain_temple_2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Das_Lakshana_%28Paryusana%29_celebrations%2C_New_York_City_Jain_temple_2.JPG/255px-Das_Lakshana_%28Paryusana%29_celebrations%2C_New_York_City_Jain_temple_2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Das_Lakshana_%28Paryusana%29_celebrations%2C_New_York_City_Jain_temple_2.JPG/340px-Das_Lakshana_%28Paryusana%29_celebrations%2C_New_York_City_Jain_temple_2.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Das_Lakshana_(Paryusana)_celebrations,_New_York_City_Jain_temple_2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Celebrating Das Lakshana (Paryushana), <a href="/wiki/Jain_Center_of_America" title="Jain Center of America">Jain Center of America</a>, New York City</div></div></div>
<p>The most important annual Jain festival is called the <i><a href="/wiki/Paryushana" title="Paryushana">Paryushana</a></i> by Svetambaras and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dasha-Lakshana-Parvan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dasha-Lakshana-Parvan (page does not exist)">Dasa lakshana parva</a></i> by the Digambaras. It is celebrated from the 12th day of the waning moon in the traditional lunisolar month of <a href="/wiki/Bhadra_(Hindu_calendar)" title="Bhadra (Hindu calendar)">Bhadrapada</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_calendar" title="Hindu calendar">Indian calendar</a>. This typically falls in August or September of the Gregorian calendar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a164,_284_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a164,_284-168">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton2011673_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton2011673-169">[164]</a></sup> It lasts eight days for Svetambaras, and ten days among the Digambaras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a164,_284_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a164,_284-168">[163]</a></sup> It is a time when lay people fast and pray. The five vows are emphasized during this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton2011673_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton2011673-169">[164]</a></sup> Svetambaras recite the <i>Kalpasūtras</i>, while Digambaras read their own texts. The festival is an occasion where Jains make active effort to stop cruelty towards other life forms, freeing animals in captivity and preventing the slaughter of animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a164,_284_168-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a164,_284-168">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="; background-color: #FFDDBB;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p><b>Forgiveness</b>
</p>
<div class="poem">
<p>I forgive all living beings,<br/>
may all living beings forgive me.<br/>
All in this world are my friends,<br/>
I have no enemies.
</p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— <i>Jain festival prayer on the last day</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a284_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a284-170">[165]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>The last day involves a focused prayer and meditation session known as <i><a href="/wiki/Samvatsari" title="Samvatsari">Samvatsari</a></i>. Jains consider this a day of atonement, granting forgiveness to others, seeking forgiveness from all living beings, physically or mentally asking for forgiveness and resolving to treat everyone in the world as friends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a164,_284_168-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a164,_284-168">[163]</a></sup> Forgiveness is asked by saying "<i><a href="/wiki/Micchami_Dukkadam" title="Micchami Dukkadam">Micchami Dukkadam</a></i>" or "<i>Khamat khamna</i>" to others. This means, "If I have offended you in any way, knowingly or unknowingly, in thought, word or action, then I seek your forgiveness." The literal meaning of <i>Paryushana</i> is "abiding" or "coming together".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort1995160_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort1995160-171">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahavir_Jayanti" title="Mahavir Jayanti">Mahavir Janma Kalyanak</a> celebrates the birth of Mahāvīra. It is celebrated on the 13th day of the lunisolar month of <a href="/wiki/Chaitra" title="Chaitra">Chaitra</a> in the traditional Indian calendar. This typically falls in March or April of the Gregorian calendar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a220_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a220-172">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah2004211_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah2004211-173">[168]</a></sup> The festivities include visiting Jain temples, <a href="/wiki/Tirtha_(Jainism)" title="Tirtha (Jainism)">pilgrimages</a> to shrines, reading Jain texts and processions of Mahāvīra by the community. At his legendary birthplace of Kundagrama in <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, north of Patna, special events are held by Jains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a220_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a220-172">[167]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Diwali_(Jainism)" title="Diwali (Jainism)">The next day of Dipawali</a> is observed by Jains as the anniversary of Mahāvīra's attainment of <i><a href="/wiki/Moksha_(Jainism)" title="Moksha (Jainism)">moksha</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPechilisRaj201386_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPechilisRaj201386-174">[169]</a></sup> The Hindu festival of <a href="/wiki/Diwali" title="Diwali">Diwali</a> is also celebrated on the same date (<i>Kartika Amavasya</i>). Jain temples, homes, offices, and shops are decorated with lights and <a href="/wiki/Diya_(lamp)" title="Diya (lamp)"><i>diyas</i></a> (small oil lamps). The lights are symbolic of knowledge or removal of ignorance. Sweets are often distributed. On Diwali morning, <i>Nirvan Ladoo</i> is offered after praying to Mahāvīra in all Jain temples across the world. The Jain new year starts right after Diwali.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPechilisRaj201386_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPechilisRaj201386-174">[169]</a></sup> Some other festivals celebrated by Jains are <a href="/wiki/Akshaya_Tritiya" title="Akshaya Tritiya">Akshaya Tritiya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raksha_Bandhan" title="Raksha Bandhan">Raksha Bandhan</a>, similar to those in the Hindu communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPechilisRaj201385_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPechilisRaj201385-175">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a164_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a164-176">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditions_and_sects">Traditions and sects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Traditions and sects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jain_schools_and_branches" title="Jain schools and branches">Jain schools and branches</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:242px;max-width:242px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:119px;max-width:119px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:177px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mahavir.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1264" data-file-width="834" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Mahavir.jpg/117px-Mahavir.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Mahavir.jpg/176px-Mahavir.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Mahavir.jpg/234px-Mahavir.jpg 2x" width="117"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Digambara <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81v%C4%ABra" title="Mahāvīra">Mahāvīra</a> iconography</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:119px;max-width:119px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:177px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shri_Simandhar_Swami.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="220" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Shri_Simandhar_Swami.jpg/117px-Shri_Simandhar_Swami.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Shri_Simandhar_Swami.jpg/176px-Shri_Simandhar_Swami.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a9/Shri_Simandhar_Swami.jpg 2x" width="117"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Śvētāmbara <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simandhar_Swami" title="Simandhar Swami">Simandhar Swami</a> iconography</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The Jain community is divided into two major <a href="/wiki/Religious_denomination" title="Religious denomination">denominations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Digambara" title="Digambara">Digambara</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Av%C4%93t%C4%81mbara" title="Śvētāmbara">Śvētāmbara</a>. Monks of the Digambara (sky-clad) tradition do not wear clothes. Female monastics of the Digambara sect wear unstitched plain white <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saree" title="Saree">sarees</a> and are referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Aryika" title="Aryika">Aryikas</a></i>. Śvētāmbara (white-clad) monastics, on the other hand, wear seamless white clothes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200245_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200245-177">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>During Chandragupta Maurya's reign, Jain tradition states that <i>Acharya</i> Bhadrabahu predicted a twelve-year-long famine and moved to Karnataka with his disciples. <a href="/wiki/Sthulabhadra" title="Sthulabhadra">Sthulabhadra</a>, a pupil of <i>Acharya</i> Bhadrabahu, is believed to have stayed in Magadha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClarkeBeyer2009326_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarkeBeyer2009326-178">[173]</a></sup> Later, as stated in tradition, when followers of <i>Acharya</i> Bhadrabahu returned, they found those who had remained at Magadha had started wearing white clothes, which was unacceptable to the others who remained naked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200247_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200247-179">[174]</a></sup> This is how Jains believe the Digambara and Śvētāmbara schism began, with the former being naked while the latter wore white clothes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192546_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192546-180">[175]</a></sup> Digambara saw this as being opposed to the Jain tenet of <i>aparigraha</i> which, according to them, required not even possession of clothes, i.e. complete nudity. In the fifth-century CE, the Council of Valabhi was organized by Śvētāmbara, which Digambara did not attend. At the council, the Śvētāmbara adopted the texts they had preserved as canonical scriptures, which Digambara has ever since rejected. This council is believed to have solidified the historic schism between these two major traditions of Jainism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrice2010104–105_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrice2010104–105-181">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFohr201521–22_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFohr201521–22-182">[177]</a></sup> The earliest record of Digambara beliefs is contained in the Prakrit <i>Suttapahuda</i> of <a href="/wiki/Kundakunda" title="Kundakunda">Kundakunda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini19913_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini19913-183">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>Digambaras and Śvētāmbara differ in their practices and dress code,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJonesRyan2007211_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJonesRyan2007211-184">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUmakant_P._Shah19875_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUmakant_P._Shah19875-185">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200231–33_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200231–33-186">[181]</a></sup> interpretations of teachings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUmakant_P._Shah19875_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUmakant_P._Shah19875-185">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini200027–28_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini200027–28-187">[182]</a></sup> and on Jain history especially concerning the <i>tirthankaras</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKailash_Chand_Jain199112_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKailash_Chand_Jain199112-188">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah200473–74_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah200473–74-189">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200221_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200221-190">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUmakant_P._Shah198717_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUmakant_P._Shah198717-191">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUmakant_P._Shah198779–80_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUmakant_P._Shah198779–80-192">[187]</a></sup> Their monasticism rules differ,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a167_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a167-193">[188]</a></sup> as does their <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a167_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a167-193">[188]</a></sup> Śvētāmbara has had more female than male mendicants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2001a47_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2001a47-194">[189]</a></sup> where Digambara has mostly had male monks<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlügel2006314–331,_353–361_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlügel2006314–331,_353–361-195">[190]</a></sup> and considers males closest to the soul's liberation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong201336–37_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong201336–37-196">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey2016182–183_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey2016182–183-197">[192]</a></sup> The Śvētāmbaras believe that women can also achieve liberation through asceticism<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey2016182–183_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey2016182–183-197">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200255–59_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200255–59-198">[193]</a></sup> and state that the 19th <i>Tirthankara</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M%C4%81ll%C4%ABn%C4%81tha" title="Māllīnātha">Māllīnātha</a> was female,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVallely200215_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVallely200215-199">[194]</a></sup> which Digambara rejects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200256_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200256-200">[195]</a></sup> Early Jain images from Mathura depict Digambara iconography until late fifth century A.D. where Svetambara iconography starts appearing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVyas199516_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVyas199516-201">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>Excavations at Mathura revealed Jain statues from the time of the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a> (c.<span class="nowrap"> </span>1st century CE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini2000167_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini2000167-202">[197]</a></sup> <i>Tirthankara</i> represented without clothes, and monks with cloth wrapped around the left arm, are identified as the <i>Ardhaphalaka</i> (half-clothed) mentioned in texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini2000167_202-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini2000167-202">[197]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yapaniya" title="Yapaniya">Yapaniyas</a>, believed to have originated from the <i>Ardhaphalaka</i>, followed Digambara nudity along with several Śvētāmbara beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini2000167_202-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini2000167-202">[197]</a></sup> In the modern era, according to Flügel, new Jain religious movements that are a "primarily devotional form of Jainism" have developed which resemble "Jain Mahayana" style devotionalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlügel2005194–243_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlügel2005194–243-203">[198]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scriptures_and_texts">Scriptures and texts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Scriptures and texts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jain_literature" title="Jain literature">Jain literature</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:80px;max-width:80px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:120px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jain_Agamas.jpeg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="1845" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Jain_Agamas.jpeg/78px-Jain_Agamas.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Jain_Agamas.jpeg/117px-Jain_Agamas.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Jain_Agamas.jpeg/156px-Jain_Agamas.jpeg 2x" width="78"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Stela depicting <i>Śhrut Jnāna</i>, or complete scriptural knowledge</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:143px;max-width:143px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:120px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Suryaprajnapati_Sutra.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="877" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Suryaprajnapati_Sutra.jpg/141px-Suryaprajnapati_Sutra.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Suryaprajnapati_Sutra.jpg/212px-Suryaprajnapati_Sutra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Suryaprajnapati_Sutra.jpg/282px-Suryaprajnapati_Sutra.jpg 2x" width="141"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The Suryaprajnaptisūtra, a fourth or third century a.C. astronomy text of Śvētāmbara Jains. Above: its manuscript from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1500</span> CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[199]</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:163px;max-width:163px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:120px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mangulam_inscription.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Mangulam_inscription.jpg/161px-Mangulam_inscription.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Mangulam_inscription.jpg/242px-Mangulam_inscription.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Mangulam_inscription.jpg/322px-Mangulam_inscription.jpg 2x" width="161"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Mangulam" title="Mangulam">Mangulam</a> inscription dated 3rd century a.C.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Jain canonical scriptures are called <i>Agamas</i>. They are believed to have been <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">verbally transmitted</a>, much like the ancient Buddhist and Hindu texts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200260–61_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200260–61-205">[200]</a></sup> and to have originated from the sermons of the <i>tirthankaras</i>, whereupon the <i><a href="/wiki/Ganadhara" title="Ganadhara">Ganadharas</a></i> (chief disciples) transmitted them as <i>Śhrut Jnāna</i> (heard knowledge).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChampat_Rai_Jain1929b135–136_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChampat_Rai_Jain1929b135–136-206">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925109–110_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925109–110-207">[202]</a></sup> The spoken scriptural language is believed to be <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ardhamagadhi" title="Ardhamagadhi">Ardhamagadhi</a></i> by the Śvētāmbara Jains, and a form of sonic resonance by the Digambara Jains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200260–61_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200260–61-205">[200]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Śvētāmbaras believe that they have preserved 45 of the 50 original Jain scriptures (having lost an Anga text and four Purva texts), while the Digambaras believe that all were lost,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200261_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200261-208">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925112–113,_121–122_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925112–113,_121–122-209">[204]</a></sup> and that Āchārya <a href="/wiki/Bhutabali" title="Bhutabali">Bhutabali</a> was the last ascetic who had partial knowledge of the original canon. According to them, Digambara <i>Āchāryas</i> recreated the oldest-known Digambara Jain texts, including the four <i>anuyoga</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain2016xii_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain2016xii-210">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini199878–81_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini199878–81-211">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925124_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925124-212">[207]</a></sup> The Digambara texts partially agree with older Śvētāmbara texts, but there are also gross differences between the texts of the two major Jain traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925121–122_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925121–122-213">[208]</a></sup> The Digambaras created a secondary canon between 600 and 900 CE, compiling it into four groups or <i>Vedas</i>: history, cosmography, philosophy and ethics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925123–124_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925123–124-214">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[f]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most popular and influential texts of Jainism have been its non-canonical literature. Of these, the <i><a href="/wiki/Kalpa_S%C5%ABtra" title="Kalpa Sūtra">Kalpa Sūtras</a></i> are particularly popular among Śvētāmbaras, which they attribute to Bhadrabahu (c. 300 a.C.). This ancient scholar is revered in the Digambara tradition, and they believe he led their migration into the ancient south Karnataka region and created their tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925125–126_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925125–126-217">[211]</a></sup> Śvētāmbaras believe instead that Bhadrabahu moved to Nepal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925125–126_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925125–126-217">[211]</a></sup> Both traditions consider his <i>Niryuktis</i> and <i>Samhitas</i> important. The earliest surviving Sanskrit text by <a href="/wiki/Umaswati" title="Umaswati">Umaswati</a>, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tattvarthas%C5%ABtra" title="Tattvarthasūtra">Tattvarthasūtra</a></i> is considered authoritative by all traditions of Jainism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJonesRyan2007439–440_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJonesRyan2007439–440-218">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2006395–396_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2006395–396-219">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[g]</a></sup> In the Digambara tradition, the texts written by Kundakunda are highly revered and have been historically influential,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinegan1989221_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinegan1989221-222">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBalcerowicz200325–34_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBalcerowicz200325–34-223">[216]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChatterjee2000282–283_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChatterjee2000282–283-224">[217]</a></sup> while the oldest being <i><a href="/wiki/Kasayapahuda" title="Kasayapahuda">Kasayapahuda</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Shatkhandagama" title="Shatkhandagama">Shatkhandagama</a></i> attributed to Acharya pushpdanta and Bhutbali. Other important Digambara Jain texts include: <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samayasara" title="Samayasara">Samayasara</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ratnakaranda_%C5%9Br%C4%81vak%C4%81c%C4%81ra" title="Ratnakaranda śrāvakācāra">Ratnakaranda śrāvakācāra</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Niyamasara" title="Niyamasara">Niyamasara</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini199132–33_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini199132–33-225">[218]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_Buddhism_and_Hinduism">Comparison with Buddhism and Hinduism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Comparison with Buddhism and Hinduism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_and_Jainism" title="Buddhism and Jainism">Buddhism and Jainism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jainism_and_Hinduism" title="Jainism and Hinduism">Jainism and Hinduism</a></div>
<p>All four <a href="/wiki/Indian_religions" title="Indian religions">Dharmic religions</a>, viz., Jainism, Hinduism, Sikhism and Buddhism, share concepts and doctrines such as karma and rebirth, with similar festivals and monastic traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomonHiggins199811–22_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomonHiggins199811–22-226">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAppleton20161–21,_25–27,_57–58,_82–84_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAppleton20161–21,_25–27,_57–58,_82–84-227">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFaul200627–28_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFaul200627–28-228">[221]</a></sup> They do not believe in eternal heaven or hell or judgment day, and leave it up to individual discretion to choose whether or not to believe in gods, to disagree with core teachings, and to choose whether to participate in prayers, rituals and festivals. They all consider values such as non-violence to be important,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShawDemy2017_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShawDemy2017-229">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">: <span class="tooltip tooltip-dashed" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed;" title='Page / location: p. 635
Quotation : "Ahiṃsā (to do no harm) is a significant aspect of three major religions: Jainism, Hinduism and Buddhism"'>p. 635</span> </sup> link suffering to craving, individual's actions, intents, and karma, and believe spirituality is a means to enlightened peace, bliss and eternal liberation (<i>moksha</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomonHiggins199818–22_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomonHiggins199818–22-230">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcFaul200627–40_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcFaul200627–40-231">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jainism differs from both Buddhism and Hinduism in its ontological premises. All believe in impermanence, but Buddhism incorporates the premise of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatta" title="Anatta">anatta</a></i> ("no eternal self or soul"). Hinduism incorporates an eternal unchanging <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atman_(Hinduism)" title="Atman (Hinduism)">atman</a></i> ("soul"), while Jainism incorporates an eternal but changing <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jiva_(Jainism)" title="Jiva (Jainism)">jiva</a></i> ("soul").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200287–88_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200287–88-232">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley20042–5_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley20042–5-233">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong2013122–125_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong2013122–125-234">[227]</a></sup> In Jain thought, there are infinite eternal <i>jivas</i>, predominantly in cycles of rebirth, and a few <i>siddhas</i> (perfected ones).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHiriyanna1993157–158,_168–169_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHiriyanna1993157–158,_168–169-235">[228]</a></sup> Unlike Jainism, Hindu philosophies encompass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advaita" title="Advaita">nondualism</a> where all souls are identical as Brahman and posited as interconnected one<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHiriyanna199354–62,_77–82,_132_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHiriyanna199354–62,_77–82,_132-236">[229]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerrett2013247–248_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerrett2013247–248-237">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartley20131–10,_76–79,_87–98_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartley20131–10,_76–79,_87–98-238">[231]</a></sup>
</p><p>While both Hinduism and Jainism believe "soul exists" to be a self-evident truth, most Hindu systems consider it to be eternally present, infinite and constant (<i><a href="/wiki/Vibhu" title="Vibhu">vibhu</a></i>), but some Hindu scholars propose soul to be atomic. Hindu thought generally discusses Atman and <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> through a monistic or dualistic framework. In contrast, Jain thought denies the Hindu metaphysical concept of Brahman, and Jain philosophy considers the soul to be ever changing and bound to the body or matter for each lifetime, thereby having a finite size that infuses the entire body of a living being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini199858,_102–105_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini199858,_102–105-239">[232]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jainism is similar to Buddhism in not recognizing the primacy of the Vedas and the Hindu Brahman. Jainism and Hinduism, however, both believe "soul exists" as a self-evident truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomonHiggins199818–22_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomonHiggins199818–22-230">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2010b174–175_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2010b174–175-240">[233]</a></sup> Jains and Hindus have frequently intermarried, particularly in northern, central and western regions of India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJuergensmeyer201154_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJuergensmeyer201154-241">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelting2009206_note_4_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelting2009206_note_4-242">[235]</a></sup> Some early colonial scholars stated that Jainism like Buddhism was, in part, a rejection of the Hindu caste system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENesfield1885116–117_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENesfield1885116–117-243">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPope188040–41_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPope188040–41-244">[237]</a></sup> but later scholars consider this a Western error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlberts2007258–259_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlberts2007258–259-245">[238]</a></sup> A caste system not based on birth has been a historic part of Jain society, and Jainism focused on transforming the individual, not society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJuergensmeyer201154_241-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJuergensmeyer201154-241">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002147–149,_304_footnote_24_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002147–149,_304_footnote_24-246">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBabb1996137–145,_54,_172_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBabb1996137–145,_54,_172-247">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESangave198073,_316–317_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESangave198073,_316–317-248">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[h]</a></sup>
</p><p>Monasticism is similar in all three traditions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnston2000681–683_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnston2000681–683-252">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaillat2003a30–34_with_footnote_28_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaillat2003a30–34_with_footnote_28-253">[245]</a></sup> with similar rules, hierarchical structure, not traveling during the four-month <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> season, and celibacy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaillat2003a30–34_with_footnote_28_253-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaillat2003a30–34_with_footnote_28-253">[245]</a></sup> originating before the Buddha or the Mahāvīra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnston2000681–683_252-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnston2000681–683-252">[244]</a></sup> Jain and Hindu monastic communities have traditionally been more mobile and had an itinerant lifestyle, while Buddhist monks have favored belonging to a <i>sangha</i> (monastery) and staying in its premises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHirakawa19934–7_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirakawa19934–7-254">[246]</a></sup> Buddhist monastic rules forbid a monk to go outside without wearing the sangha's distinctive ruddy robe, or to use wooden bowls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnston2000681–683_252-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnston2000681–683-252">[244]</a></sup> In contrast, Jain monastic rules have either required nakedness (Digambara) or white clothes (Śvētāmbara), and they have disagreed on the legitimacy of the wooden or empty gourd as the begging bowl by Jain monks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnston2000681–683_252-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnston2000681–683-252">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[i]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jains have similar views with Hindus that violence in self-defence can be justified,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[248]</a></sup> and that a soldier who kills enemies in combat is performing a legitimate duty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[249]</a></sup> Jain communities accepted the use of military power for their defence; there were Jain monarchs, military commanders, and soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[250]</a></sup> The Jain and Hindu communities have often been very close and mutually accepting. Some Hindu temples have included a Jain <i>Tirthankara</i> within its premises in a place of honour,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong20095–6_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong20095–6-260">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharmaGhosal2006100–103_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharmaGhosal2006100–103-261">[252]</a></sup> while temple complexes such as the <a href="/wiki/Badami_cave_temples" title="Badami cave temples">Badami cave temples</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khajuraho" title="Khajuraho">Khajuraho</a> feature both Hindu and Jain monuments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichell201438–52,_60–61_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichell201438–52,_60–61-262">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERingWatsonSchellinger1996468–470_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERingWatsonSchellinger1996468–470-263">[254]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_architecture">Art and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Art and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jain_art" title="Jain art">Jain art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_a_leaf_with_the_birth_of_mahavira.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2378" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Detail_of_a_leaf_with_the_birth_of_mahavira.jpg/170px-Detail_of_a_leaf_with_the_birth_of_mahavira.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Detail_of_a_leaf_with_the_birth_of_mahavira.jpg/255px-Detail_of_a_leaf_with_the_birth_of_mahavira.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Detail_of_a_leaf_with_the_birth_of_mahavira.jpg/340px-Detail_of_a_leaf_with_the_birth_of_mahavira.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_a_leaf_with_the_birth_of_mahavira.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The birth of Mahavira, from the <i><a href="/wiki/Kalpa_S%C5%ABtra" title="Kalpa Sūtra">Kalpa Sūtra</a></i> (c.<span class="nowrap"> </span>1375–1400 CE)</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:322px;max-width:322px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:130px;max-width:130px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:139px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/%22Sihanamdika_ayagapata%22%2C_Jain_votive_plate%2C_Kankali_Tila%2C_Mathura_dated_25-50_CE.jpg/128px-%22Sihanamdika_ayagapata%22%2C_Jain_votive_plate%2C_Kankali_Tila%2C_Mathura_dated_25-50_CE.jpg" 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/%22Sihanamdika_ayagapata%22%2C_Jain_votive_plate%2C_Kankali_Tila%2C_Mathura_dated_25-50_CE.jpg/256px-%22Sihanamdika_ayagapata%22%2C_Jain_votive_plate%2C_Kankali_Tila%2C_Mathura_dated_25-50_CE.jpg 2x" width="128"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Ayagapata" title="Ayagapata">Sihanamdika ayagapata</a>, 25-50 CE, <a href="/wiki/Kankali_Tila" title="Kankali Tila">Kankali Tila</a>, Mathura, <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:188px;max-width:188px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:139px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KHANDAGIRI_AND_UDAYGIRI_Cave_Inscriptions_1.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/KHANDAGIRI_AND_UDAYGIRI_Cave_Inscriptions_1.jpg/186px-KHANDAGIRI_AND_UDAYGIRI_Cave_Inscriptions_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/KHANDAGIRI_AND_UDAYGIRI_Cave_Inscriptions_1.jpg/279px-KHANDAGIRI_AND_UDAYGIRI_Cave_Inscriptions_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/KHANDAGIRI_AND_UDAYGIRI_Cave_Inscriptions_1.jpg/372px-KHANDAGIRI_AND_UDAYGIRI_Cave_Inscriptions_1.jpg 2x" width="186"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Inscriptions at a Udaygiri-Khandagiri 2nd-1st-century a.C. Jain rock cut cave, <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[255]</a></sup></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Jainism has contributed significantly to Indian art and architecture. Jain arts depict life legends of <i>tirthankara</i> or other important people, particularly with them in a seated or standing meditative posture. <i>Yakshas</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Yakshini" title="Yakshini">yakshinis</a></i>, attendant spirits who guard the <i>tirthankara</i>, are usually shown with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah1998184_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah1998184-265">[256]</a></sup> The earliest known Jain image is in the <a href="/wiki/Patna" title="Patna">Patna</a> museum. It is dated approximately to the third century a.C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah1998184_265-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah1998184-265">[256]</a></sup> Bronze images of Pārśva can be seen in the Prince of Wales Museum, Mumbai, and in the Patna museum; these are dated to the second century a.C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUmakant_P._Shah198795_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUmakant_P._Shah198795-266">[257]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Ayagapata</i> is a type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Votive" title="Votive">votive</a> tablet used in Jainism for donation and worship in the early centuries. These tablets are decorated with objects and designs central to Jain worship such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Stupa" title="Stupa">stupa</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dharmacakra" title="Dharmacakra">dharmacakra</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Triratna" title="Triratna">triratna</a></i>. They present simultaneous trends or image and symbol worship. Numerous such stone tablets were discovered during excavations at ancient Jain sites like <a href="/wiki/Kankali_Tila" title="Kankali Tila">Kankali Tila</a> near <a href="/wiki/Mathura" title="Mathura">Mathura</a> in Uttar Pradesh, India. The practice of donating these tablets is documented from first century a.C. to third century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKishore201517–43_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKishore201517–43-267">[258]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJainFischer19789–10_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJainFischer19789–10-268">[259]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Samavasarana" title="Samavasarana">Samavasarana</a></i>, a preaching hall of <i>tirthankaras</i> with various beings concentrically placed, is an important theme of Jain art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley2009184_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley2009184-269">[260]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kirti_Stambh" title="Kirti Stambh">Jain tower in Chittor</a>, Rajasthan, is a good example of Jain architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen2012a1–2_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen2012a1–2-270">[261]</a></sup> Decorated manuscripts are preserved in Jain libraries, containing diagrams from Jain cosmology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah1998183_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah1998183-271">[262]</a></sup> Most of the paintings and illustrations depict historical events, known as <i>Panch Kalyanaka</i>, from the life of the <i>tirthankara</i>. Rishabha, the first <i>tirthankara</i>, is usually depicted in either the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_position" title="Lotus position">lotus position</a> or <i>kayotsarga</i>, the standing position. He is distinguished from other <i>tirthankara</i> by the long locks of hair falling to his shoulders. Bull images also appear in his sculptures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah1998113_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah1998113-272">[263]</a></sup> In paintings, incidents from his life, like his marriage and <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a> marking his forehead, are depicted. Other paintings show him presenting a pottery bowl to his followers; he is also seen painting a house, weaving, and being visited by his mother Marudevi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJainFischer197816_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJainFischer197816-273">[264]</a></sup> Each of the twenty-four <i>tirthankara</i> is associated with distinctive emblems, which are listed in such texts as <i>Tiloyapannati</i>, <i>Kahavaali</i> and <i>Pravacanasaarodhara</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah1998187_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah1998187-274">[265]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples">Temples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Temples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jain_temple" title="Jain temple">Jain temple</a></div>

class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chitharal" title="Chitharal">Chitharal</a></div></div></div></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:India_location_map.svg" title="File:India location map.svg">class=notpageimage| </a></div>Major pilgrimage and temple sites in Jainism.</div></div></div>
<p>A Jain temple, a <i>Derasar</i> or <i>Basadi</i>, is a <a href="/wiki/Place_of_worship" title="Place of worship">place of worship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBabb199666_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBabb199666-275">[266]</a></sup> Temples contain <i>tirthankara</i> images, some fixed, others moveable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBabb199666_275-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBabb199666-275">[266]</a></sup> These are stationed in the inner sanctum, one of the two sacred zones, the other being the main hall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBabb199666_275-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBabb199666-275">[266]</a></sup> One of the images is marked as the <i>moolnayak</i> (primary deity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBabb199668_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBabb199668-276">[267]</a></sup> A <i><a href="/wiki/Manastambha" title="Manastambha">manastambha</a></i> (column of honor) is a pillar that is often constructed in front of Jain temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESettar1989195_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESettar1989195-277">[268]</a></sup> Temple construction is considered a meritorious act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESangave2001188_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESangave2001188-278">[269]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ancient Jain monuments include the Udaigiri Hills near Bhelsa (<a href="/wiki/Vidisha" title="Vidisha">Vidisha</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Pataini_temple" title="Pataini temple">Pataini temple</a> in Madhya Pradesh, the <a href="/wiki/Ellora_Caves" title="Ellora Caves">Ellora</a> in Maharashtra, the <a href="/wiki/Palitana_temples" title="Palitana temples">Palitana temples</a> in Gujarat, and the Jain temples at Dilwara Temples near <a href="/wiki/Mount_Abu" title="Mount Abu">Mount Abu</a>, Rajasthan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[270]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[271]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ranakpur_Jain_temple" title="Ranakpur Jain temple">Chaumukha temple</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ranakpur" title="Ranakpur">Ranakpur</a> is considered one of the most beautiful Jain temples and is famous for its detailed carvings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESehdev_Kumar2001106_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESehdev_Kumar2001106-281">[272]</a></sup> According to Jain texts, <a href="/wiki/Shikharji" title="Shikharji">Shikharji</a> is the place where twenty of the twenty-four Jain <i>Tīrthaṅkaras</i> along with many other monks attained <i>moksha</i> (died without being reborn, with their soul in <i><a href="/wiki/Siddhashila" title="Siddhashila">Siddhashila</a></i>). The Shikharji site in northeastern <a href="/wiki/Jharkhand" title="Jharkhand">Jharkhand</a> is therefore a revered pilgrimage site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2010130–133_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2010130–133-282">[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[j]</a></sup> The Palitana temples are the holiest shrine for the Śvētāmbara Murtipujaka sect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBerger2010352_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBerger2010352-285">[275]</a></sup> Along with Shikharji the two sites are considered the holiest of all pilgrimage sites by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain_community" title="Jain community">Jain community</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[276]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain_complex,_Khajuraho" title="Jain complex, Khajuraho">Jain complex, Khajuraho</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pattadakal#Jain_Temple" title="Pattadakal">Jain Narayana temple</a> are part of a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[277]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[278]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shravanabelagola" title="Shravanabelagola">Shravanabelagola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saavira_Kambada_Basadi" title="Saavira Kambada Basadi">Saavira Kambada Basadi</a> or <i>1000 pillars</i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahma_Jinalaya" title="Brahma Jinalaya">Brahma Jinalaya</a> are important Jain centers in Karnataka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButaliaSmall2004367_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButaliaSmall2004367-289">[279]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFergusson1876271_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFergusson1876271-290">[280]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPandya201417_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPandya201417-291">[281]</a></sup> In and around <a href="/wiki/Madurai" title="Madurai">Madurai</a>, there are 26 caves, 200 stone beds, 60 inscriptions, and over 100 sculptures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[282]</a></sup>
</p><p>The second–first century a.C. Udayagiri and Khandagiri Caves are rich with carvings of <i>tirthanakars</i> and deities with inscriptions including the <a href="/wiki/Hathigumpha_inscription" title="Hathigumpha inscription">Elephant Cave inscription</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpinder_Singh2016460_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpinder_Singh2016460-294">[284]</a></sup> Jain cave temples at <a href="/wiki/Badami_cave_temples" title="Badami cave temples">Badami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mangi-Tungi" title="Mangi-Tungi">Mangi-Tungi</a> and the Ellora Caves are considered important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen2012a50_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen2012a50-295">[285]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sittanavasal_Cave" title="Sittanavasal Cave">Sittanavasal Cave</a> temple is a fine example of Jain art with an early cave shelter, and a medieval rock-cut temple with excellent fresco paintings comparable to Ajantha. Inside are seventeen stone beds with second century a.C. <a href="/wiki/Tamil-Brahmi" title="Tamil-Brahmi">Tamil-Brahmi</a> inscriptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[286]</a></sup> The eighth century <a href="/wiki/Kalugumalai_Jain_Beds" title="Kalugumalai Jain Beds">Kazhugumalai temple</a> marks the revival of Jainism in South India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[287]</a></sup>
</p>
