<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pilgrimages">Pilgrimages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Pilgrimages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tirtha_(Jainism)" title="Tirtha (Jainism)">Tirtha (Jainism)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shikharji_Parasnath_Giridih.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Shikharji_Parasnath_Giridih.jpg/170px-Shikharji_Parasnath_Giridih.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Shikharji_Parasnath_Giridih.jpg/255px-Shikharji_Parasnath_Giridih.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Shikharji_Parasnath_Giridih.jpg/340px-Shikharji_Parasnath_Giridih.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shikharji_Parasnath_Giridih.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Shikharji" title="Shikharji">Shikharji</a></div></div></div>
<p>Jain <i>Tirtha</i> (pilgrim) sites are divided into the following categories:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETitze1998_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETitze1998-298">[288]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Siddhakshetra</i> – Site of the <a href="/wiki/Moksha_(Jainism)" title="Moksha (Jainism)"><i>moksha</i></a> of an <i>arihant</i> (<i>kevalin</i>) or <i>tirthankara</i>, such as: <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kailash" title="Mount Kailash">Ashtapada</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rishabhanatha" title="Rishabhanatha">Rishabhanatha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shikharji" title="Shikharji">Shikharji</a> of 20 <a href="/wiki/Tirthankara" title="Tirthankara">Tirthankara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Girnar" title="Girnar">Girnar</a> of <a href="/wiki/Neminatha" title="Neminatha">Neminatha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pawapuri" title="Pawapuri">Pawapuri</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahaveera" title="Mahaveera">Mahaveera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Champapuri" title="Champapuri">Champapuri</a> (capital of <a href="/wiki/Anga" title="Anga">Anga</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Vasupujya" title="Vasupujya">Vasupujya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mangi-Tungi" title="Mangi-Tungi">Mangi-Tungi</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rama" title="Rama">Ram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palitana" title="Palitana">Palitana</a> of 3 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pandavas" title="Pandavas">Pandavas</a>.</li>
<li><i>Atishayakshetra</i> – Locations where divine events are believed to have occurred, such as: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahavirji" title="Mahavirji">Mahavirji</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rishabhdeo" title="Rishabhdeo">Rishabhdeo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kundalpur" title="Kundalpur">Kundalpur</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tijara_Jain_Temple" title="Tijara Jain Temple">Tijara</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aharji" title="Aharji">Aharji</a>.</li>
<li><i>Puranakshetra</i> –  Places associated with the lives of great men, such as: <a href="/wiki/Ayodhya" title="Ayodhya">Ayodhya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vidisha" title="Vidisha">Vidisha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hastinapur" title="Hastinapur">Hastinapur</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rajgir" title="Rajgir">Rajgir</a>.</li>
<li><i>Gyanakshetra</i> –  Places associated with famous <i><a href="/wiki/Acharya" title="Acharya">acharyas</a></i>, or centers of learning, such as <a href="/wiki/Shravanabelagola" title="Shravanabelagola">Shravanabelagola</a>.</li></ul>
<p>Outside contemporary India, Jain communities built <a href="/wiki/Nagarparkar_Jain_Temples" title="Nagarparkar Jain Temples">temples</a> in locations such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nagarparkar" title="Nagarparkar">Nagarparkar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a> (Pakistan). However, according to a UNESCO tentative world heritage site application, Nagarparkar was not a "major religious centre or a place of pilgrimage" for Jainism, but it was once an important cultural landscape before "the last remaining Jain community left the area in 1947 at Partition".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-299">[289]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statues_and_sculptures">Statues and sculptures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Statues and sculptures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jain_sculpture" title="Jain sculpture">Jain sculpture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thirthankara_Suparshvanath_Museum_Rietberg_RVI_306.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2403" data-file-width="2152" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Thirthankara_Suparshvanath_Museum_Rietberg_RVI_306.jpg/170px-Thirthankara_Suparshvanath_Museum_Rietberg_RVI_306.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Thirthankara_Suparshvanath_Museum_Rietberg_RVI_306.jpg/255px-Thirthankara_Suparshvanath_Museum_Rietberg_RVI_306.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Thirthankara_Suparshvanath_Museum_Rietberg_RVI_306.jpg/340px-Thirthankara_Suparshvanath_Museum_Rietberg_RVI_306.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thirthankara_Suparshvanath_Museum_Rietberg_RVI_306.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Idol of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supar%C5%9Bvan%C4%81tha" title="Suparśvanātha">Suparśvanātha</a></div></div></div>
<p>Jain sculptures usually depict one of the twenty-four <i>tīrthaṅkaras</i>; Parshvanatha, Rishabhanatha and Mahāvīra are among the more popular, often seated in <a href="/wiki/Lotus_position" title="Lotus position">lotus position</a> or <i>kayotsarga</i>, along with <i>Arihant</i>, <a href="/wiki/Bahubali" title="Bahubali">Bahubali</a>, and protector deities like <a href="/wiki/Ambika_(Jainism)" title="Ambika (Jainism)">Ambika</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArora2007405_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArora2007405-300">[290]</a></sup> Quadruple images are also popular. <i>Tirthankar</i> idols look similar, differentiated by their individual symbol, except for Parshvanatha whose head is crowned by a snake. Digambara images are naked without any beautification, whereas Śvētāmbara depictions are clothed and ornamented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2010184_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2010184-301">[291]</a></sup>
</p><p>A monolithic, 18-metre (59-foot) statue of Bahubali, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gommateshvara" title="Gommateshvara">Gommateshvara</a></i>, built in 981 CE by the <a href="/wiki/Western_Ganga_dynasty" title="Western Ganga dynasty">Ganga</a> minister and commander <a href="/wiki/Chavundaraya" title="Chavundaraya">Chavundaraya</a>, is situated on a hilltop in <a href="/wiki/Shravanabelagola" title="Shravanabelagola">Shravanabelagola</a> in <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>. This statue was voted first in the SMS poll Seven Wonders of India conducted by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times_of_India" title="The Times of India">The Times of India</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[292]</a></sup> The 33-metre (108-foot) tall <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statue_of_Ahi%E1%B9%83s%C4%81" title="Statue of Ahiṃsā">Statue of Ahiṃsā</a> (depicting Rishabhanatha) was erected in the <a href="/wiki/Nashik_district" title="Nashik district">Nashik district</a> in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[293]</a></sup> Idols are often made in <i><a href="/wiki/Ashtadhatu" title="Ashtadhatu">Ashtadhatu</a></i> (literally "eight metals"), namely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akota_Bronze" title="Akota Bronze">Akota Bronze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>, stone <a href="/wiki/Monolith" title="Monolith">monoliths</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_cut" title="Rock cut">rock cut</a>, and precious stones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPratapaditya_Pal198622_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPratapaditya_Pal198622-304">[294]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[295]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols">Symbols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Symbols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jain_symbols" title="Jain symbols">Jain symbols</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:267px;max-width:267px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:92px;max-width:92px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:112px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Om_ja%C3%AFn_orange.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="208" data-file-width="167" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Om_ja%C3%AFn_orange.svg/90px-Om_ja%C3%AFn_orange.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Om_ja%C3%AFn_orange.svg/135px-Om_ja%C3%AFn_orange.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Om_ja%C3%AFn_orange.svg/180px-Om_ja%C3%AFn_orange.svg.png 2x" width="90"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Om#Jainism" title="Om">Om in Jainism</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:171px;max-width:171px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:112px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jain_flag.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="460" data-file-width="688" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Jain_flag.jpg/169px-Jain_flag.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Jain_flag.jpg/254px-Jain_flag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Jain_flag.jpg/338px-Jain_flag.jpg 2x" width="169"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Jain_flag" title="Jain flag">Jain flag</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Jain icons and arts incorporate symbols such as the <a href="/wiki/Swastika" title="Swastika">swastika</a>, <a href="/wiki/Om#Jainism" title="Om"><i>Om</i></a>, and the <i>Ashtamangala</i>. In Jainism, <i>Om</i> is a condensed reference to the initials "A-A-A-U-M" of the five parameshthis: "<i>Arihant</i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ashiri" title="Ashiri">Ashiri</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Acharya" title="Acharya">Acharya</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upajjhaya" title="Upajjhaya">Upajjhaya</a></i>, <i>Muni</i>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[296]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925410–411_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp1925410–411-307">[297]</a></sup> or the five lines of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namokara_Mantra" title="Namokara Mantra"><i>Ṇamōkāra</i> Mantra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAgarwal2012135_308-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAgarwal2012135-308">[298]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAgarwal201380_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAgarwal201380-309">[299]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Ashtamangala" title="Ashtamangala">Ashtamangala</a></i> is a set of eight auspicious symbols:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETitze1998234_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETitze1998234-310">[300]</a></sup> in the Digambara tradition, these are <a href="/wiki/Chatra_(umbrella)" title="Chatra (umbrella)">chatra</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dhvaja" title="Dhvaja">dhvaja</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Kalasha" title="Kalasha">kalasha</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Fly-whisk" title="Fly-whisk">fly-whisk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mirror" title="Mirror">mirror</a>, chair, <a href="/wiki/Hand_fan" title="Hand fan">hand fan</a> and vessel. In the Śvētāmbar tradition, they are Swastika, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Srivatsa" title="Srivatsa">Srivatsa</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Nandavarta" title="Nandavarta">Nandavarta</a></i>, <i>Vardhmanaka</i> (food vessel), <i>Bhadrasana</i> (seat), <i><a href="/wiki/Kalasha" title="Kalasha">Kalasha</a></i> (pot), <i>Darpan</i> (mirror) and pair of fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETitze1998234_310-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETitze1998234-310">[300]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stambha_of_Parshwanath_Jain_Mandir,_Belgachhiya_18.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5472" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Stambha_of_Parshwanath_Jain_Mandir%2C_Belgachhiya_18.jpg/220px-Stambha_of_Parshwanath_Jain_Mandir%2C_Belgachhiya_18.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Stambha_of_Parshwanath_Jain_Mandir%2C_Belgachhiya_18.jpg/330px-Stambha_of_Parshwanath_Jain_Mandir%2C_Belgachhiya_18.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Stambha_of_Parshwanath_Jain_Mandir%2C_Belgachhiya_18.jpg/440px-Stambha_of_Parshwanath_Jain_Mandir%2C_Belgachhiya_18.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stambha_of_Parshwanath_Jain_Mandir,_Belgachhiya_18.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A symbol to represent the Jain community was chosen in 1975 as part of the commemoration of the 2,500th anniversary of Mahavira’s <i>nirvana</i>.</div></div></div>
<p>The hand with a wheel on the palm symbolizes <a href="/wiki/Ahimsa_in_Jainism" title="Ahimsa in Jainism"><i>ahimsā</i></a>. The wheel represents the <i><a href="/wiki/Dharmachakra" title="Dharmachakra">dharmachakra</a></i>, which stands for the resolve to halt the <i>saṃsāra</i> (wandering) through the relentless pursuit of <i>ahimsā</i>. The five colours of the <a href="/wiki/Jain_flag" title="Jain flag">Jain flag</a> represent the <i><a href="/wiki/Pa%C3%B1ca-Parame%E1%B9%A3%E1%B9%ADhi" title="Pañca-Parameṣṭhi">Pañca-Parameṣṭhi</a></i> and the five vows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain2012iv_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVijay_K._Jain2012iv-311">[301]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Swastika" title="Swastika">swastika</a>'s four arms symbolise the four realms in which rebirth occurs according to Jainism: humans, heavenly beings, hellish beings and non-humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2001a17_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2001a17-312">[302]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJansmaJain2006123_313-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJansmaJain2006123-313">[303]</a></sup> The three dots on the top represent the three jewels mentioned in ancient texts: correct faith, correct understanding and correct conduct, believed to lead to spiritual perfection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2001a17–18_314-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2001a17–18-314">[304]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1974, on the 2500th anniversary of the nirvana of Mahāvīra, the Jain community chose a single combined image for Jainism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson2006225_315-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson2006225-315">[305]</a></sup> It depicts the three <i>lokas</i>, heaven, the human world and hell. The semi-circular topmost portion symbolizes <i>Siddhashila</i>, a zone beyond the three realms. The Jain swastika and the symbol of <i>Ahiṃsā</i> are included, with the Jain mantra <i><a href="/wiki/Parasparopagraho_Jivanam" title="Parasparopagraho Jivanam">Parasparopagraho Jīvānām</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESangave2001123_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESangave2001123-316">[306]</a></sup> from sūtra 5.21 of Umaswati's <i>Tattvarthasūtra</i>, meaning "souls render service to one another".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVallely2013358_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVallely2013358-317">[307]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Jainism" title="History of Jainism">History of Jainism</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a chronological guide, see <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Jainism" title="Timeline of Jainism">Timeline of Jainism</a>.</div>
<p>Jainism is a religion founded in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_India" title="Ancient India">ancient India</a>. Jains trace their history through twenty-four <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tirthankars" title="Tirthankars">tirthankars</a></i> and revere <a href="/wiki/Rishabhanatha" title="Rishabhanatha">Rishabhanatha</a> as the first <i>tirthankara</i> (in the present time-cycle). Some artifacts found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilisation" title="Indus Valley civilisation">Indus River Valley civilization</a> have been suggested as a link to ancient Jain culture, but very little is known about the Indus Valley iconography and script. The last two <i>tirthankars</i>, the 23rd <i>tirthankara</i> <a href="/wiki/Parshvanatha" title="Parshvanatha">Parshvanatha</a> (c. 9th–8th century BCE) and the 24th <i>tirthankara</i> <a href="/wiki/Mahavira" title="Mahavira">Mahavira</a> (<abbr>c.</abbr> 599 – c. 527 BCE) are considered historical figures. Mahavira was a contemporary of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a>. According to Jain texts, the 22nd <i>Tirthankara</i> <a href="/wiki/Neminatha" title="Neminatha">Neminatha</a> lived about 85,000 years ago and was the cousin of <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[308]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Photo_of_lord_adinath_bhagwan_at_kundalpur.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Photo_of_lord_adinath_bhagwan_at_kundalpur.JPG/170px-Photo_of_lord_adinath_bhagwan_at_kundalpur.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Photo_of_lord_adinath_bhagwan_at_kundalpur.JPG/255px-Photo_of_lord_adinath_bhagwan_at_kundalpur.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Photo_of_lord_adinath_bhagwan_at_kundalpur.JPG/340px-Photo_of_lord_adinath_bhagwan_at_kundalpur.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Photo_of_lord_adinath_bhagwan_at_kundalpur.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rishabhanatha" title="Rishabhanatha">Rishabhdev</a>, believed to have lived over 592.704×10<sup>18</sup> years ago, is considered the traditional founder of Jainism.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient">Ancient</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Ancient">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Jainism" title="Timeline of Jainism">Timeline of Jainism</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Arama%E1%B9%87a" title="Śramaṇa">Śramaṇa</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" 2x" width="162"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><i>Chaumukha</i> Sculpture with Four Jinas (Rishabhanatha (Adinatha), Parshvanatha, Neminatha, and Mahavira), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LACMA" title="LACMA">LACMA</a>, sixth century</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Jainism is an ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_religion" title="Indian religion">Indian religion</a> of obscure origins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESangave2001185_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESangave2001185-319">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERankinMardia2013975_320-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERankinMardia2013975-320">[310]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192513_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192513-321">[311]</a></sup> Jains claim it to be eternal, and consider the first <i>tirthankara</i> <a href="/wiki/Rishabhanatha" title="Rishabhanatha">Rishabhanatha</a> as the reinforcer of Jain Dharma in the current time cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192516_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192516-322">[312]</a></sup> It is one of the <i><a href="/wiki/%C5%9Arama%E1%B9%87a" title="Śramaṇa">Śramaṇa</a></i> traditions of ancient India, those that rejected the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veda" title="Veda">Vedas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELochtefeld2002b639_323-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELochtefeld2002b639-323">[313]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBillimoria19881–30_324-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBillimoria19881–30-324">[314]</a></sup> and according to the twentieth-century scholar of comparative religion <a href="/wiki/Sarvepalli_Radhakrishnan" title="Sarvepalli Radhakrishnan">Dr. Sarvepalli Radhakrishnan</a>, Jainism was in existence before the Vedas were composed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJambuvijaya2002114_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJambuvijaya2002114-325">[315]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPande1957353_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPande1957353-326">[316]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[k]</a></sup>
</p><p>The historicity of first twenty two Tirthankaras is not determined yet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESangave2001104,_129_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESangave2001104,_129-329">[318]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaraswati1908444_330-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaraswati1908444-330">[319]</a></sup> The 23rd Tirthankara, <a href="/wiki/Parshvanatha" title="Parshvanatha">Parshvanatha</a>, was a historical being,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZimmer1953183_331-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZimmer1953183-331">[320]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini199810_332-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini199810-332">[321]</a></sup> dated by the Jain tradition to the ninth century BCE;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnett19577_333-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnett19577-333">[322]</a></sup> historians date him to the eighth or seventh century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[323]</a></sup> Mahāvīra is considered a contemporary of the Buddha, in around the sixth century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2003a383_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2003a383-335">[324]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeownPrebish2013127–130_336-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeownPrebish2013127–130-336">[325]</a></sup> The interaction between the two religions began with the Buddha;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESangave2001105_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESangave2001105-337">[326]</a></sup> later, they competed for followers and the merchant trade networks that sustained them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeelis201072–76_338-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeelis201072–76-338">[327]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHirakawa19934–7_254-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirakawa19934–7-254">[246]</a></sup> Buddhist and Jain texts sometimes have the same or similar titles but present different doctrines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQvarnström2003ix–xi,_151–162_339-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQvarnström2003ix–xi,_151–162-339">[328]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Kings <a href="/wiki/Bimbisara" title="Bimbisara">Bimbisara</a> (c. 558–491 BCE), <a href="/wiki/Ajatashatru" title="Ajatashatru">Ajatashatru</a> (c. 492–460 BCE), and <a href="/wiki/Udayin" title="Udayin">Udayin</a> (c. 460–440 BCE) of the <a href="/wiki/Haryanka_dynasty" title="Haryanka dynasty">Haryanka dynasty</a> were patrons of Jainism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192541_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192541-340">[329]</a></sup> Jain tradition states that <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_Maurya" title="Chandragupta Maurya">Chandragupta Maurya</a> (322–298 BCE), the founder of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mauryan_Empire" title="Mauryan Empire">Mauryan Empire</a> and grandfather of <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a>, became a monk and disciple of Jain ascetic <a href="/wiki/Bhadrabahu" title="Bhadrabahu">Bhadrabahu</a> in the later part of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKulkeRothermund200463–65_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKulkeRothermund200463–65-341">[330]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoesche20037–18_342-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoesche20037–18-342">[331]</a></sup> Jain texts state that he died intentionally at Shravanabelagola by fasting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKulkeRothermund200463–65_341-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKulkeRothermund200463–65-341">[330]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji198839–46,_234–236_343-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji198839–46,_234–236-343">[332]</a></sup> Versions of Chandragupta's story appear in Buddhist, Jain, and Hindu texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMookerji19884–21,_232–237_344-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMookerji19884–21,_232–237-344">[333]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192542_345-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192542-345">[334]</a></sup><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:322px;max-width:322px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:161px;max-width:161px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:106px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Udayagiri_Caves_-_Rani_Gumpha_01.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3368" data-file-width="5053" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Udayagiri_Caves_-_Rani_Gumpha_01.jpg/159px-Udayagiri_Caves_-_Rani_Gumpha_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Udayagiri_Caves_-_Rani_Gumpha_01.jpg/239px-Udayagiri_Caves_-_Rani_Gumpha_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Udayagiri_Caves_-_Rani_Gumpha_01.jpg/318px-Udayagiri_Caves_-_Rani_Gumpha_01.jpg 2x" width="159"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Udayagiri_and_Khandagiri_Caves" title="Udayagiri and Khandagiri Caves">Udayagiri and Khandagiri Caves</a> built by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Kharavela" title="King Kharavela">King Kharavela</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mahameghavahana_dynasty" title="Mahameghavahana dynasty">Mahameghavahana dynasty</a> in second century a.C.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:157px;max-width:157px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:106px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_grotte_Jain_Indra_Sabha_Ellora_Caves,_India.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1754" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/La_grotte_Jain_Indra_Sabha_Ellora_Caves%2C_India.jpg/155px-La_grotte_Jain_Indra_Sabha_Ellora_Caves%2C_India.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/La_grotte_Jain_Indra_Sabha_Ellora_Caves%2C_India.jpg/233px-La_grotte_Jain_Indra_Sabha_Ellora_Caves%2C_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/La_grotte_Jain_Indra_Sabha_Ellora_Caves%2C_India.jpg/310px-La_grotte_Jain_Indra_Sabha_Ellora_Caves%2C_India.jpg 2x" width="155"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The Indra Sabha cave at the <a href="/wiki/Ellora_Caves" title="Ellora Caves">Ellora Caves</a>, are co-located with Hindu and Buddhist monuments.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The third century BC emperor Ashoka, in his pillar edicts, mentions the <i>Niganthas</i> (Jains).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192543_346-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192543-346">[335]</a></sup> <i>Tirthankara</i> statues date back to the second century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpinder_Singh2016444_347-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpinder_Singh2016444-347">[336]</a></sup> Archeological evidence suggests that Mathura was an important Jain center from the second century BC. onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJainFischer19789–10_268-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJainFischer19789–10-268">[259]</a></sup> Inscriptions from as early as the first century CE already show the schism between Digambara and Śvētāmbara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192549_348-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192549-348">[337]</a></sup> There is inscriptional evidence for the presence of Jain monks in south India by the second or first centuries BCE, and archaeological evidence of Jain monks in Saurashtra in Gujarat by the second century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort2010202_349-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort2010202-349">[338]</a></sup>
</p><p>Royal patronage has been a key factor in the growth and decline of Jainism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah200469–70_350-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah200469–70-350">[339]</a></sup> In the second half of the first century CE, Hindu kings of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rashtrakuta" title="Rashtrakuta">Rashtrakuta</a> dynasty sponsored major Jain cave temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPereira197721–24_351-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPereira197721–24-351">[340]</a></sup> King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harshavardhana" title="Harshavardhana">Harshavardhana</a> of the seventh century championed Jainism, Buddhism and all traditions of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192552_352-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192552-352">[341]</a></sup> The Pallava King <a href="/wiki/Mahendravarman_I" title="Mahendravarman I">Mahendravarman I</a> (600–630 CE) converted from Jainism to Shaivism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELochtefeld2002a409_353-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELochtefeld2002a409-353">[342]</a></sup> His work <i><a href="/wiki/Mattavilasa_Prahasana" title="Mattavilasa Prahasana">Mattavilasa Prahasana</a></i> ridicules certain Shaiva sects and the Buddhists and expresses contempt for Jain ascetics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArunachalam1981170_354-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArunachalam1981170-354">[343]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Seuna_(Yadava)_dynasty" title="Seuna (Yadava) dynasty">Yadava dynasty</a> built many temples at the <a href="/wiki/Ellora_Caves" title="Ellora Caves">Ellora Caves</a> between 700 and 1000 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[344]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGopal1990178_356-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGopal1990178-356">[345]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen2012b1–10_357-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen2012b1–10-357">[346]</a></sup> King <a href="/wiki/%C4%80ma" title="Āma">Āma</a> of the eighth century converted to Jainism, and the Jain pilgrimage tradition was well established in his era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192552–54_358-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192552–54-358">[347]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mularaja" title="Mularaja">Mularaja</a> (10th century CE), the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Chalukya_dynasty" title="Chalukya dynasty">Chalukya dynasty</a>, constructed a Jain temple, even though he was not a Jain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192556_359-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192556-359">[348]</a></sup> During the 11th century, <a href="/wiki/Basava" title="Basava">Basava</a>, a minister to the Jain <a href="/wiki/Kalachuris_of_Kalyani" title="Kalachuris of Kalyani">Kalachuri</a> king <a href="/wiki/Bijjala_II" title="Bijjala II">Bijjala</a>, converted many Jains to the <a href="/wiki/Lingayatism" title="Lingayatism">Lingayat</a> Shaivite sect. The Lingayats destroyed Jain temples and adapted them to their use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192575–77_360-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glasenapp192575–77-360">[349]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoysala_Dynasty" title="Hoysala Dynasty">Hoysala</a> King <a href="/wiki/Vishnuvardhana" title="Vishnuvardhana">Vishnuvardhana</a> (c. 1108–1152 CE) became a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vaishnava" title="Vaishnava">Vaishnavite</a> under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Ramanuja" title="Ramanuja">Ramanuja</a>, and Vaishnavism then grew rapidly in what is now Karnataka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDas2005161_361-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDas2005161-361">[350]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval">Medieval</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Medieval">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gori_Mandar.jpg"><img alt="Jain monuments in Nagarparkar, Pakistan" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Gori_Mandar.jpg/220px-Gori_Mandar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Gori_Mandar.jpg/330px-Gori_Mandar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Gori_Mandar.jpg/440px-Gori_Mandar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gori_Mandar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The ruins of <a href="/wiki/Gori_Temple,_Nagarparkar" title="Gori Temple, Nagarparkar">Gori Jain temples</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nagarparkar" title="Nagarparkar">Nagarparkar</a>, Pakistan, a pilgrimage site before 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_299-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-299">[289]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Jainism faced persecution during and after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests_on_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="Muslim conquests on the Indian subcontinent">Muslim conquests on the Indian subcontinent</a>. The scholarship in context of Jain relations with the ruler of <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> remains scarce, notwithstanding there were several instances of cordial relations of Jains with prominent rulers of the Sultanate. <a href="/wiki/Alauddin_Khalji" title="Alauddin Khalji">Alauddin Khalji</a> (1296-1316), as attested by the Jain texts held discussions with Jain sages and once specially summoned Acharya Mahasena to Delhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[351]</a></sup> One more prominent Jain figure Acharya Ramachandra Suri was also honored by him. During his reign, his governor of Gujarat, <a href="/wiki/Alp_Khan" title="Alp Khan">Alp Khan</a> permitted the reconstruction of the temples razed during earlier Muslim conquests and himself made huge donation for the renovation of Jain temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pushpa_363-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pushpa-363">[352]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-364"><a href="#cite_note-364">[353]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_bin_Tughluq" title="Muhammad bin Tughluq">Muhammad bin Tughluq</a> (1325-1351) according to the Jain chronicles favoured the Jain scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-365"><a href="#cite_note-365">[354]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal emperors</a> in general were influenced by the Jain scholars and made patronage and grants for their pilgrimage sites under <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a> (1540-1556), <a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a> (1556-1605), <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a> (1605–1627) and even <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a> (1658-1707).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-366"><a href="#cite_note-366">[355]</a></sup> Despite this, there were instances of religious bigotry during the Mughal rule towards Jains. <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> (1526-1530), the first Mughal emperor ordered the destruction of various Jain idols in <a href="/wiki/Gwalior" title="Gwalior">Gwalior</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-367"><a href="#cite_note-367">[356]</a></sup> In 1567, Akbar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Chittorgarh_(1567-1568)" title="Siege of Chittorgarh (1567-1568)">ravaged Chittor (capital of the Sisodias)</a>. After the conquest of the fort, Akbar ordered the destruction of several Jain shrines and temples in Chittor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-368"><a href="#cite_note-368">[357]</a></sup> Similarly there were instances of desecration of Jain religious shrines under <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a> and most notably under <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-369"><a href="#cite_note-369">[358]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Jain community were the traditional bankers and financiers, and this significantly impacted the Muslim rulers. However, they rarely were a part of the political power during the Islamic rule period of the Indian subcontinent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort199885–86_370-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort199885–86-370">[359]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_era">Colonial era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Colonial era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:327px;max-width:327px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:135px;max-width:135px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:151px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Virchand_Gandhi_poster.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1785" data-file-width="1569" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Virchand_Gandhi_poster.jpg/133px-Virchand_Gandhi_poster.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Virchand_Gandhi_poster.jpg/200px-Virchand_Gandhi_poster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Virchand_Gandhi_poster.jpg/266px-Virchand_Gandhi_poster.jpg 2x" width="133"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A poster of <a href="/wiki/Virchand_Gandhi" title="Virchand Gandhi">Virchand Gandhi</a> who represented Jainism at the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_World%27s_Religions" title="Parliament of the World's Religions">Parliament of the World's Religions</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> in 1893.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:188px;max-width:188px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:151px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shrimad_Rajchandra_Spiritual_Centre_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="281" data-file-width="345" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Shrimad_Rajchandra_Spiritual_Centre_%28cropped%29.jpg/186px-Shrimad_Rajchandra_Spiritual_Centre_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Shrimad_Rajchandra_Spiritual_Centre_%28cropped%29.jpg/279px-Shrimad_Rajchandra_Spiritual_Centre_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6d/Shrimad_Rajchandra_Spiritual_Centre_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="186"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A 34 feet tall idol of <a href="/wiki/Shrimad_Rajchandra" title="Shrimad Rajchandra">Shrimad Rajchandra</a> at <a href="/wiki/Dharampur,_Gujarat" title="Dharampur, Gujarat">Dharampur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valsad_district" title="Valsad district">Valsad</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>A Gujarati Jain scholar <a href="/wiki/Virchand_Gandhi" title="Virchand Gandhi">Virchand Gandhi</a> represented Jainism at the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Parliament_of_Religions" title="World Parliament of Religions">World Parliament of Religions</a> in 1893, held in America during the <a href="/wiki/World%27s_Columbian_Exposition" title="World's Columbian Exposition">Chicago World's Fair</a>. He worked to defend the rights of Jains, and wrote and lectured extensively on Jainism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-it_371-0"><a href="#cite_note-it-371">[360]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OpenCourt_372-0"><a href="#cite_note-OpenCourt-372">[361]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Shrimad_Rajchandra" title="Shrimad Rajchandra">Shrimad Rajchandra</a>, a mystic, poet and philosopher revered amongst some Jains in <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a> is believed to have attained <i>jatismaran gnana</i> (ability to recollect past lives) at the age of seven. Virchand Gandhi mentioned this feat at the Parliament of the World's Religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KarbhariGāndhī1911_373-0"><a href="#cite_note-KarbhariGāndhī1911-373">[362]</a></sup> 
He is best known because of his association with Mahatma Gandhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESalter2002145_374-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESalter2002145-374">[363]</a></sup> They were introduced in Mumbai in 1891 and had various conversations through letters while Gandhi was in South Africa. Gandhi noted his impression of Shrimad Rajchandra in his autobiography, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_My_Experiments_with_Truth" title="The Story of My Experiments with Truth">The Story of My Experiments with Truth</a></i>, calling him his "guide and helper" and his "refuge in moments of spiritual crisis". Shrimad Rajchandra composed <a href="/wiki/Atma_Siddhi" title="Atma Siddhi">Shri Atmasiddhi Shastra</a>, considered his magnum opus, containing the essence of Jainism in a single sitting of 1.5–2 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-375"><a href="#cite_note-375">[364]</a></sup> He expounds on the 6 fundamental truths of the soul:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-376"><a href="#cite_note-376">[365]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Self (soul) exists</li>
<li>It is permanent and eternal</li>
<li>It the doer of its own actions</li>
<li>It is the enjoyer or the sufferer of its actions</li>
<li>Liberation exists</li>
<li>There is a path to achieve liberation.</li></ol>
<p>Colonial era reports and Christian missions variously viewed Jainism as a sect of Hinduism, a sect of Buddhism, or a distinct religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-377"><a href="#cite_note-377">[366]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkins1902283_378-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkins1902283-378">[367]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESunavala193491–93_379-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESunavala193491–93-379">[368]</a></sup> Christian missionaries were frustrated at Jain people without pagan creator gods refusing to convert to Christianity, while colonial era Jain scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Champat_Rai_Jain" title="Champat Rai Jain">Champat Rai Jain</a> defended Jainism against criticism and misrepresentation by Christian activists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini200033_380-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini200033-380">[369]</a></sup> Missionaries of Christianity and Islam considered Jain traditions idolatrous and superstitious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHackett200863–68_381-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackett200863–68-381">[370]</a></sup> These criticisms, states <a href="/wiki/John_E._Cort" title="John E. Cort">John E. Cort</a>, were flawed and ignored similar practices within sects of Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECort201012–16,_200–207,_218–219,_251_with_note_10_382-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECort201012–16,_200–207,_218–219,_251_with_note_10-382">[371]</a></sup>
</p><p>The British colonial government in India and Indian princely states promoted religious tolerance. However, laws were passed that made roaming naked by anyone an arrestable crime. This drew popular support from the majority Hindu population, but particularly impacted Digambara monks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlügel2006348–349_383-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlügel2006348–349-383">[372]</a></sup> The Akhil Bharatiya Jain Samaj opposed this law, claiming that it interfered with Jain religious rights. Acharya <a href="/wiki/Shantisagar" title="Shantisagar">Shantisagar</a> entered Bombay (now Mumbai) in 1927, but was forced to cover his body. He then led an India-wide tour as the naked monk with his followers, to various Digambara sacred sites, and was welcomed by kings of the Maharashtra provinces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlügel2006348–349_383-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlügel2006348–349-383">[372]</a></sup> Shantisagar fasted to oppose the restrictions imposed on Digambara monks by the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a> and prompted their discontinuance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah200456_384-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENatubhai_Shah200456-384">[373]</a></sup> The laws were abolished by India after independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlügel2006359–360_385-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlügel2006359–360-385">[374]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">Modern era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Modern era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jain_communities" title="Jain communities">Jain communities</a></div>
<p>Followers of Jainism are called "Jains", a word derived from the Sanskrit <i>jina</i> (victor), which means an omniscient person who teaches the path of salvation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpinder_Singh2016313_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpinder_Singh2016313-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESangave200615_386-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESangave200615-386">[375]</a></sup> The majority of Jains currently reside in India. With four to five million followers worldwide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVoorst201496_387-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVoorst201496-387">[376]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeltonBaumann2010lix,_1395_388-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeltonBaumann2010lix,_1395-388">[377]</a></sup> Jainism is small compared to <a href="/wiki/Major_religious_groups" title="Major religious groups">major world religions</a>. Jains form 0.37% of <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_India" title="Jainism in India">India</a>'s population, mostly in the states of <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_Maharashtra" title="Jainism in Maharashtra">Maharashtra</a> (1.4 million in 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jaindemographics_389-0"><a href="#cite_note-jaindemographics-389">[378]</a></sup> 31.46% of Indian Jains), <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_Rajasthan" title="Jainism in Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a> (13.97%), <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_Gujarat" title="Jainism in Gujarat">Gujarat</a> (13.02%) and <a href="/wiki/Madhya_Pradesh" title="Madhya Pradesh">Madhya Pradesh</a> (12.74%). Significant Jain populations exist in <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_Karnataka" title="Jainism in Karnataka">Karnataka</a> (9.89%), <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_Uttar_Pradesh" title="Jainism in Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a> (4.79%), <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_Delhi" title="Jainism in Delhi">Delhi</a> (3.73%) and <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Jain" title="Tamil Jain">Tamil Nadu</a> (2.01%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jaindemographics_389-1"><a href="#cite_note-jaindemographics-389">[378]</a></sup> Outside India, Jain communities can be found in most areas hosting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-resident_Indian_and_person_of_Indian_origin" title="Non-resident Indian and person of Indian origin">large Indian populations</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_Europe" title="Jainism in Europe">Europe</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Jainism in the United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_the_United_States" title="Jainism in the United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_Canada" title="Jainism in Canada">Canada</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley200943_390-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley200943-390">[379]</a></sup> Australia and <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_Africa" title="Jainism in Africa">Kenya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMugambi2010108_391-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMugambi2010108-391">[380]</a></sup> Jainism also counts several non-Indian converts; for example, it is spreading rapidly in <a href="/wiki/Jainism_in_Japan" title="Jainism in Japan">Japan</a>, where more than 5,000 families have converted between 2010 and 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-392"><a href="#cite_note-392">[381]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/National_Family_Health_Survey" title="National Family Health Survey">National Family Health Survey</a> (NFHS-4) conducted in 2015–16, Jains form the wealthiest community in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-393"><a href="#cite_note-393">[382]</a></sup> According to its 2011 census, they have the country's highest literacy rate (87%) among those aged seven and older, and the most college graduates;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-394"><a href="#cite_note-394">[383]</a></sup> excluding the retired, Jain literacy in India exceeded 97%. The female to male sex ratio among Jains is .940; among Indians in the 0–6 year age range the ratio was second lowest (870 girls per 1,000 boys), higher only than <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhs</a>. Jain males have the highest work participation rates in India, while Jain females have the lowest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-395"><a href="#cite_note-395">[384]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jainism has been praised for some of its practices and beliefs. Greatly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Shrimad_Rajchandra" title="Shrimad Rajchandra">Shrimad Rajchandra</a>, the leader of the campaign for Indian independence, <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> stated regarding Jainism:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERudolphRudolph1984171_396-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERudolphRudolph1984171-396">[385]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>No religion in the World has explained the principle of <i>Ahiṃsā</i> so deeply and systematically as is discussed with its applicability in every human life in Jainism. As and when the benevolent principle of <i>Ahiṃsā</i> or non-violence will be ascribed for practice by the people of the world to achieve their end of life in this world and beyond, Jainism is sure to have the uppermost status and Mahāvīra is sure to be respected as the greatest authority on <i>Ahiṃsā</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJanardan_Pandey199850_397-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJanardan_Pandey199850-397">[386]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Acharya_Chandana" title="Acharya Chandana">Chandanaji</a> became the first Jain woman to receive the title of <a href="/wiki/Acharya" title="Acharya">Acharya</a> in 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillerLongReading2019[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_9i_DwAAQBAJpgPA10_7,_10–]_398-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillerLongReading2019[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_9i_DwAAQBAJpgPA10_7,_10–]-398">[387]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1093669538">.mw-parser-output .portalbox{padding:0}.mw-parser-output .portalborder{border:solid #aaa 1px}.mw-parser-output .portalbox.tleft{margin:0.5em 1em 0.5em 0}.mw-parser-output .portalbox.tright{margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul{display:table;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:175px;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .portalborder>ul{padding:0.1em;background:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li{display:table-row}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li>span:first-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li>span:last-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle}</style><div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint plainlist portalbox portalborder tright" role="navigation">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This view, however, is not shared by all Jain sub-traditions. For example, the Terapanthi Jain tradition, with about 250,000 followers, considers both good karma such as compassionate charity, and bad karma such as sin, as binding one's soul to worldly morality. It states that any karma leads to a negation of the "absolute non-violence" principle, given man's limited perspective. It recommends that the monk or nun seeking salvation must avoid hurting or helping any being in any form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlügel20021266–1267_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlügel20021266–1267-64">[64]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jain literature, like Buddhist and Hindu literature, has also debated the aspects of violence and non-violence in food creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlson20141–7_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlson20141–7-76">[75]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In Jainism, the <i>ahiṃsā</i> precept for a mendicant requires avoidance of touching or disturbing any living being including plants. It also mandates never swimming in water, nor lighting or fire or extinguish one, nor thrashing arms in the air as such actions can torment or hurt other beings that live in those states of matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2008892–894_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2008892–894-71">[70]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-136"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-136">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first is desavakasika (staying in a restrained surrounding, cutting down worldly activities). The third is <i>posadhopavasa</i> (fasting on the 8th and 14th days on lunar waxing and waning cycles). The fourth is <i>dana</i> (giving alms to Jain monks, nuns or spiritual people).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJaini1998180–181_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJaini1998180–181-135">[132]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Dundas, <i>samayika</i> seems to have meant "correct behavior" in early Jainism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas2002170_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas2002170-141">[137]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-216"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-216">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Not to be confused with the four <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veda" title="Veda">Vedas</a></i> of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a164–165_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalal2010a164–165-215">[210]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-221"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-221">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">That Which Is, known as the <i>Tattvartha Sūtra</i> to Jains, is recognized by all four Jain traditions as the earliest, most authoritative and comprehensive summary of their religion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUmāsvāti1994xi–xiii_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUmāsvāti1994xi–xiii-220">[214]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-251"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-251">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to <a href="/wiki/Richard_Gombrich" title="Richard Gombrich">Richard Gombrich</a> and other scholars, Buddhism too was not a rejection or rebellion against any ancient caste system and it too was focused on individual's liberation from rebirths and suffering. The caste system in Buddhist societies and monasteries outside India have been documented. Gombrich states, "Some modernists go so far as to say that the Buddha was against caste altogether: this is not the case, but is one of the mistakes picked up from western authors."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich2012344–353_with_footnotes_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich2012344–353_with_footnotes-249">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlberts2007258–259_245-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlberts2007258–259-245">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlorida2005134–137_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlorida2005134–137-250">[243]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-256"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-256">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Whether the begging utensils of a monk, such as robe and begging bowl, were justified and legitimate for a Jain monk and were not considered an impediment on the path to salvation, remained a bone of contention among various splinter groups within Jainism, and was partly responsible for the ultimate Digambara-Svetambara split, although it would be a grave oversimplification to reduce the roots of the split to just monks' robes and bowls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBalcerowicz201542–43_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBalcerowicz201542–43-255">[247]</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-284"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-284">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some texts refer to the place as Mount Sammeta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobi1964275_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobi1964275-283">[274]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-328"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-328">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Long notes that <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=%E1%B9%9A%E1%B9%A3abha&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ṛṣabha (page does not exist)">Ṛṣabha</a>, the first Tīrthaṅkara of Jainism, means "bull," and that images of bulls are found at seals from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilization" title="Indus Valley civilization">Indus Valley civilization</a>, speculating that they may be related to Jainism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong201353–54_327-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong201353–54-327">[317]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jainism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Citations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width" style="column-width: 23em;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Long, Jeffery D. (2013), <i>Jainism: An Introduction</i>, I.B.
